Faith and Disbelief
intermediate
8 min
The Core Argument: Impossibility of Obliging Abu Lahab
Argument that obligating Abu Lahab to believe specific news is impossible.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why obligating Abu Lahab to believe he will not believe is impossible due to the necessary contradiction between his existing belief and the required belief.

Core Idea
Obliging someone to believe what contradicts their own certain knowledge is logically impossible.

In this passage, the author presents a careful theological argument about the limits of obligation (taklif). He uses the case of Abu Lahab to show that if someone already has certain inner knowledge of their own belief, they cannot be validly obliged to believe the opposite. This lesson walks through that argument and the distinction between general and detailed belief that resolves an objection.

Guided Path
1
Read the argument and objection
Start by reading the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice how the author sets up the impossibility and then an objection is raised.
Focus on the logical flow: premise, objection, response.
2
Identify the two types of belief
The response distinguishes between general belief (tasdiq ijmail) and detailed belief (tasdiq tafsili). See how each applies to Abu Lahab.
General belief is possible; detailed belief in this specific report leads to contradiction.
3
Understand why detailed belief is impossible
The author argues that detailed belief would require combining belief and disbelief in one state. Ask yourself: why can't Abu Lahab simply change his belief?
The key is that he has necessary knowledge of his current belief, not just a changeable opinion.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وهو ) أي تصديقه بعد تصديقه مع كونه مصدقاً مستمراً تصديق بما علم من خلافه ضرورة)، أي إذا كان مصدقاً كان عالماً بتصديقه علماً ضرورياً وجدانياً فلا يمكنه حينئذ التصديق بعدم التصديق لأنه يجد في باطنه خلافه وهو التصديق بل يكون علمه بتصديقه موجباً لتكذيبه في الأخبار بأنه لا يصدق، (وأنه) أي إيمانه المشتمل على ما ذكر (محال) لاستلزامه الجمع بل التصديق والتكذيب في حالة واحدة، وإذا كان المكلف به محالاً لم يكن للتكليف بإتيانه فائدة، واعترض عليه بأن الإيمان واجب بما علم مجيئه به لا بما جاء به مطلقا سواء علمه المكلف أو لم يعلمه ولا نسلم أن هذا الخبر مما علم أبو لهب مجيئه به حتى يلزم تصديقه فيه، وتلخيصه أن الإيمان هو التصديق الإجمالي أي كل ما جاء به فهو حق وليس في هذا التصديق الإجمالي من أبي لهب استحالة وأما التصديق التفصيلي منه فهو مشروط هذا الخبر ومستلزم للجمع بين النقيضين فهو المحال دون الأول فليتأمل . قوله : ( لأنه تعالى أخبر بأنه لا يؤمن ليس في قوله تعالى : سيصلي ناراً ذات لهب المسد :٣]، ما يدل على ذلك لجواز أن يكون صليها للفسق على أن يحتمل أن يحمل الإيمان المنفي على إيمان الموافاة، اللهم إلا أن يستفاد أنه لا يؤمن أصلا من خبر الرسول عليه السلام وإسناد الأخبار إلى الله تعالى لأن الرسول عليه السلام ما ينطق عن الهوى إن هو إلا وحي يوحى . التصديق قوله : (وهو تصديق بما علم من نفسه خلافه ضرورة وأنه (محال) لا يخفى أن مجرد التصديق بما علم من نفسه خلافه ليس بمحال ألا ترى أن من اعتقد أن العالم قديم مأمور بأن يصدق بأنه حادث ويبدل اعتقاده إليه ولا استحالة فيه مع أنه تصديق بما وجد من نفسه خلافه، بل الاستحالة فيما نحن فيه إنما نشأ من خصوصية المقام حيث لزم فيه الجمع بين وعدمه، وهو محال بالضرورة وذلك لأن الإيمان بأنه لا يؤمن يتوقف على ثبوت عدم الإيمان فيه لأن الإيمان وعدمه من أفعال القلب، والتصديق بفعل القلب يتوقف على ثبوته في القلب فيلزم من وقوعه المكلف به الجمع بين الإيمان وعدمه . بين قوله : ( ولا نسلم أن هذا الخبر مما علم أبو لهب (مجيئه قيل : لا شك أن سماع هذا الخبر ممكن له وأنه مكلف بالتصديق التفصيلي على تقدير سماعه فيلزم منه جواز التكليف بالجمع النقيضين وإن لم يقع وهو المطلوب ،هاهنا، ولعل هذا هو وجه التأمل ولك أن تقول : لا نسلم أنه مكلف بالتصديق التفصيلي على تقدير العلم بهذا الخبر ويؤيده ما قال أن الحاجب من أن المكلف إذا علم أن المكلف به لا يقع لا يجوز تكليفه به فإن قلت : إذا لم يكلف لزم أن لا يعاقب والقول بأن التكليف أولاً كاف لا جهة له لأن الساقط لا يعود قلت : المعلوم يقينا معاقب بالفعل لعدم إتيانه بالتصديق الإجمالي الذي أمر به وأما أنه كان يعاقب على ذلك التقدير أيضاً فيحتاج إلى البيان، فليتأمل .
And it (i.e., his believing after his believing while he is continuously a believer is believing what he knows from himself to be contrary necessarily), i.e., if he is a believer he knows his belief by necessary and immediate knowledge, so he cannot then believe in non-belief because he finds in his inner self the opposite of it, which is belief; rather his knowledge of his belief necessitates his disbelieving the report that he does not believe. And it (i.e., his faith comprising what was mentioned) is impossible because it entails combining belief and disbelief in one state. And if what one is obligated to do is impossible, then the obligation to perform it has no benefit. And an objection was raised: faith is obligatory in what one knows he (the Prophet) brought, not in what he brought absolutely whether the obligated person knows it or not, and we do not concede that this report is among what Abu Lahab knew he brought, so that his believing it would be necessary. And its summary: faith is general belief, i.e., everything he brought is true, and in this general belief from Abu Lahab there is no impossibility. As for detailed belief from him, it is conditional on this report and entails combining two opposites, so it is impossible, unlike the first. So let it be considered. His statement: (Because He, exalted, informed that he will not believe. There is nothing in His statement 'He will roast in a flaming fire' [al-Masad 111:3] indicating that, because it is possible that he roasts in it for sin, and also it is possible that the negated faith is interpreted as the faith of deathbed compliance. Unless it is inferred that he will not believe at all from the report of the Messenger, peace be upon him, and the attribution of reports to God because the Messenger does not speak from desire; it is nothing but revelation revealed. Belief. His statement: (And it is believing what he knows from himself to be contrary necessarily, and it is impossible). It is not hidden that mere believing what one knows from himself to be contrary is not impossible. Do you not see that one who believes the world is eternal is commanded to believe that it is originated and change his belief, and there is no impossibility in that, even though it is believing what he found in himself to be contrary. Rather, the impossibility in our case arises from the specificity of the situation, where it necessitates combining [belief] and its absence, and that is impossible by necessity. That is because believing that he will not believe depends on the existence of non-belief in him, since belief and non-belief are acts of the heart, and believing an act of the heart depends on its existence in the heart, so from its occurrence as the obligated act follows the combining of belief and non-belief in him. Between: His statement: (And we do not concede that this report is among what Abu Lahab knew [its coming]. It was said: There is no doubt that hearing this report is possible for him and that he is obliged to detailed belief on the assumption of hearing it, so it follows from it the permissibility of obligating the combination of two opposites, even if it does not occur, and that is the objective here. Perhaps this is the aspect of consideration. And you may say: We do not concede that he is obliged to detailed belief on the assumption of knowledge of this report, and it is supported by what was said: the preventer is that if the obligated person knows that the obligated act will not occur, it is not permissible to obligate him with it. For if you say: If he is not obligated, it follows that he will not be punished, and the statement that obligation is sufficient first has no justification because what has fallen does not return. I say: It is known with certainty that he is punished in actuality for not performing the general belief he was commanded to do. As for whether he would be punished on that assumption also, that needs explanation. So let it be considered.
Source pages p. 175

Commentary
This passage is an argument with objection and response concerning the impossibility of obligating Abu Lahab to specifically believe that he will not believe. The author begins by explaining that if Abu Lahab is already a believer, he has necessary inner knowledge of his own belief; thus he cannot genuinely believe that he does not believe, because that would require him to simultaneously hold belief and disbelief, which is impossible (muhal). Consequently, any obligation to perform such an act is futile. An objection is then raised: faith is only required in what one knows the Prophet brought in general, not in every particular report, and it is not conceded that Abu Lahab knew this particular report. The response distinguishes between general belief (tasdiq ijmail) and detailed belief (tasdiq tafsili). General belief—accepting all that the Prophet brought as true—is possible and not impossible. Detailed belief in this specific report, however, would require combining belief with the knowledge of one's own contrary state (i.e., one's actual belief), leading to a contradiction. The passage then presents further considerations: first, the verse about Abu Lahab may not explicitly state his unbelief in this world; second, mere believing what one knows to be contrary is not always impossible (as when a person changes a false belief). The impossibility here is due to the specific context where the act of believing necessarily entails the simultaneous existence of both belief and its absence. The discussion concludes by debating whether obligation is effective when the obligated person knows the act cannot occur, with a final note that punishment is certain for neglecting general belief but may be uncertain for the detailed impossible obligation.

Key Terms
تصديق إجمالي
تصديق تفصيلي
تكليف
محال
جمع النقيضين

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That the impossibility arises from the mere act of believing something contrary to one's prior belief, which is actually possible (as when changing a false belief).
Correction: The impossibility here is specific: it requires combining belief and disbelief in the same moment, not just changing beliefs over time.

Checkpoint
Why does the author say that obligating Abu Lahab to believe he will not believe is impossible?
A) Because Abu Lahab is not obligated to believe any particular report.
B) Because it would require him to combine belief and disbelief simultaneously, which is impossible.
C) Because the news is not authentically from the Prophet.
D) Because general belief is sufficient and detailed belief is never required.

Key Takeaway
General belief (tasdiq ijmail) is always possible, but detailed belief (tasdiq tafsili) in a report that contradicts one's certain inner knowledge is impossible because it entails combining two opposites.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you know something for certain in your heart, how do you react when someone demands you believe the opposite? What does this lesson reveal about the integrity of inner conviction?
?
Consider an area where your general faith (tasdiq ijmaly) is firm, but you struggle with detailed belief in specific teachings. How does the distinction between general and detailed belief help you approach that struggle without hypocrisy?
?
This lesson argues that obliging someone to believe what contradicts their certain knowledge is futile. Have you ever expected someone to believe something against their deep-seated knowledge? How can you instead invite them to reconsider with honesty?

Faith and Disbelief

Clarifications on Impossibility and Obligation
Clarifications on the impossibility and the validity of obligation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the nuanced nature of impossibility in this case: it is not due to mere contradiction but to the specific condition of combining belief and its absence in the same moment, and you will grasp the debate about whether obligation is valid when the act is known impossible.

Core Idea
Impossibility here is contextual, not absolute; obligation is futile when the act cannot occur.

This lesson continues the discussion by addressing further clarifications. The author first responds to a potential misinterpretation of the Quranic verse about Abu Lahab. Then he elaborates that mere belief contrary to one's current belief is not impossible per se, but the specific situation here requires combining belief and its absence. Finally, a debate ensues about whether an obligation can be valid when the obligated person knows the act will not occur, with implications for punishment.

Guided Path
1
Understand the verse clarification
Read the part about the verse from Surah al-Masad. Why does the author say it does not explicitly prove Abu Lahab's unbelief in this world?
The verse says 'He will roast in a fire,' which could be for sin, not necessarily for disbelief.
2
See why impossibility is contextual
The author gives an example of someone changing a false belief. Why is that possible but our case is not?
In belief change, the old belief is abandoned; in our case, the required belief must coexist with the existing certain knowledge.
3
Follow the debate on obligation
The final part discusses whether obliging someone to do the impossible is valid. Notice the arguments about punishment.
Key point: if the act is known impossible, some say obligation is invalid; others say it is valid but punishment is for failing the possible part (general belief).

Commentary
This passage is an argument with objection and response concerning the impossibility of obligating Abu Lahab to specifically believe that he will not believe. The author begins by explaining that if Abu Lahab is already a believer, he has necessary inner knowledge of his own belief; thus he cannot genuinely believe that he does not believe, because that would require him to simultaneously hold belief and disbelief, which is impossible (muhal). Consequently, any obligation to perform such an act is futile. An objection is then raised: faith is only required in what one knows the Prophet brought in general, not in every particular report, and it is not conceded that Abu Lahab knew this particular report. The response distinguishes between general belief (tasdiq ijmail) and detailed belief (tasdiq tafsili). General belief—accepting all that the Prophet brought as true—is possible and not impossible. Detailed belief in this specific report, however, would require combining belief with the knowledge of one's own contrary state (i.e., one's actual belief), leading to a contradiction. The passage then presents further considerations: first, the verse about Abu Lahab may not explicitly state his unbelief in this world; second, mere believing what one knows to be contrary is not always impossible (as when a person changes a false belief). The impossibility here is due to the specific context where the act of believing necessarily entails the simultaneous existence of both belief and its absence. The discussion concludes by debating whether obligation is effective when the obligated person knows the act cannot occur, with a final note that punishment is certain for neglecting general belief but may be uncertain for the detailed impossible obligation.

Checkpoint
Why does the author say that mere belief contrary to one's current knowledge is not always impossible?
A) Because people can change their beliefs over time.
B) Because such belief does not require combining two opposites simultaneously.
C) Because Abu Lahab is a special case.
D) Because the Quran allows it.

Key Takeaway
The impossibility in obligating Abu Lahab to detailed belief is contextual: it requires the simultaneous existence of belief and non-belief in the heart, which is impossible, unlike simple belief change.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you hear a command from Allah that seems impossible for you to fulfill, do you use that as an excuse to neglect it, or do you examine whether the impossibility is real? This passage shows that mere difficulty or change of belief is not true impossibility. What command have you been avoiding because you assumed it was impossible for you? What would it take for you to sincerely strive for it, trusting that Allah only obligates what is possible in principle?
?
The objector in the passage argued that hearing the report obligates detailed belief, but the responder denied it because the obligated person knows the act will not occur. Reflect on your own heart: Is there a truth you know from the Quran and Sunnah but you act as if it will never be required of you? Why do you hold back? What would it mean to actually submit to that truth, even if it is difficult?
?
The passage emphasizes that the Prophet, peace be upon him, does not speak from desire; his words are revelation. Do you treat the Prophet's commands as absolute revelation, or do you find yourself rationalizing exceptions like the objector? In what specific area of your life are you hesitating to obey a clear prophetic teaching because you think the obligation is impossible or unreasonable? How does this passage challenge that hesitation?

Faith and Disbelief

The Basran Mu'tazilite Argument for Reward
The author introduces the Mu'tazilite rational basis for reward.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the rational basis for the Mu'tazilite claim that God must reward obedience to avoid absurdity.

Core Idea
Without reward, God's commands would be pointless—and absurdity is impossible.

In this passage, the author introduces the fifth section on reward and punishment and lays out the core Mu'tazilite argument from reason. The Basran Mu'tazila argue that burdensome obligations must serve some purpose, and that purpose is our benefit through reward. If there were no reward, the obligations would be purposeless, which is absurd. This short but powerful argument reveals how the Mu'tazilites ground theology in the principle that God must act wisely and avoid the absurd.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage carefully
Read the Arabic text once, then the literal translation. Note the key terms: ثواب (reward), عقاب (punishment), معتزلة (Mu'tazila), عبث (absurdity).
The passage is dense; take your time with the translation.
2
Identify the two options for God's obligations
The Basran Mu'tazila present a dilemma: either the burdensome obligations have a purpose or they do not. If they do not, that is absurd (qabīḥ). If they do, that purpose must be our benefit, which requires reward.
Notice how they exclude the possibility of God acting without purpose.
3
Reflect on the conclusion
Why does the Mu'tazilite claim that reward is obligatory on God? Because if God commands hardship without compensating reward, the command is purposeless—and the Mu'tazilites hold that God cannot commit an absurd act.
This is not about human rights but about the necessity of rational consistency in divine actions.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المقصد الخامس : النظر في الثواب والعقاب ] في فروع للمعتزلة على أصلهم في حكم العقل بحسن الأفعال وقبحها والإيجاب على الله والنظر هاهنا في الثواب والعقاب، لا في أمور أخر أوجبوها عليه ( أما الثواب فأوجبه معتزلة البصرة لأن التكاليف الشاقة ليست إلا لنفعنا وهو بالثواب عليها بيانه (أنها أي تلك التكاليف إما) لا لغرض وهو عبث قبيح) قوله: (وجب هلاك (أكلها يجوز أن يقال : فليكن أكلها يوم الجزاء وذاتها الآن لكن لما ورد على بقاء ذاتها أيضاً قوله تعالى: كل شيء هالك إلا وجهه ) [ القصص : ٨٨] لم يلتفت إلى هذا القول وأجيب بما يقطع دار الشبهة. قوله : ( لامتناع تداخل الأجسام ولأنا لسنا الآن في الجنة بالاتفاق فلو كان السماء والأرض عرض الجنة الآن لكنا فيه الآن .
The fifth section: Consideration of Reward and Punishment. [This is] on branches of the Mu'tazilites based on their principle of reason's judgment of the goodness and badness of actions and obligation upon God, and the consideration here is on reward and punishment, not on other matters which they have made obligatory upon Him. As for reward, the Mu'tazilites of Basra made it obligatory because burdensome obligations are only for our benefit, and He [rewards] for them. Its explanation: Those obligations are either not for a purpose, which is vile absurdity, or for a purpose. His statement: 'the fruit would necessarily perish' — it may be said: let its fruit be on the Day of Recompense and its essence now, but since the verse 'Everything is perishing except His Face' also came regarding the permanence of its essence, this statement was not heeded, and it was answered with what cuts off the core of the doubt. His statement: 'due to the impossibility of interpenetration of bodies' — and because we are not now in Paradise by agreement, so if the heavens and earth were the width of Paradise now, we would be in it now.
Source pages p. 330

Commentary
This passage introduces the fifth section of the book on reward and punishment (thawāb wa ʿiqāb) within the context of Mu'tazilite theology. It states that the Mu'tazila base their doctrines on reason's independent judgment of good and evil and the necessity of God acting according to wisdom. The Basran Mu'tazila specifically argue that reward is obligatory because God's commands are for our benefit, and without reward they would be purposeless, which is absurd. The passage then includes two brief comments that seem to be later glosses on the earlier arguments about Paradise: one responding to the objection about the fruit of Paradise, suggesting that perhaps the fruit exists now but its essence will be on the Day of Judgment, but this is rejected because the verse 'Everything perishes' applies to its essence; the other comment notes that if the width of Paradise were literally the heavens and earth, we would be inside it now, which we are not.

Correction: The argument is based on the necessity of avoiding absurdity in God's actions, not on any human entitlement.

Definition of Repentance (Tawbah)
Defines repentance linguistically as returning and legally as regret with determination.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the linguistic and legal definitions of repentance, including the conditions of regret and determination, and the nuance regarding motivation.

Core Idea
True repentance is regret for a sin because it is a sin, not for its worldly harm.

Repentance is a central concept in Islamic theology. This lesson from Sanusi's work clarifies its precise meaning. We will explore the linguistic root of 'returning' and the legal criteria that distinguish genuine repentance from mere regret.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once and then the translation to grasp the full flow of the definition and commentary.
Notice that the passage begins with a linguistic definition before moving to the legal one.
2
Identify the two definitions
Find the linguistic definition ('returning') and the legal definition ('regret for a sin...'). Note how they differ.
The legal definition includes explicit conditions.
3
Break down the legal definition components
The legal definition has three key parts: regret, the sin being the object of regret, and determination not to return. Understand each condition.
The passage explains why regret for non-sins does not count.
4
Reflect on the motivation discussion
Consider the question about repentance motivated by fear of Hell or hope of Paradise. The passage concludes that it is still valid because the regret is due to sinfulness.
This refines the nuance of pure repentance.
5
Summarize the takeaway
In your own words, state the essence of repentance according to this passage.
Ensure you incorporate both the conditions and the motivation aspect.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
في التوبة وفيه بحثان: الأول : في حقيتها وهي في اللغة : الرجوع قال الله تعالى ثم تاب عليهم ليتوبوا [التوبة : 118]، أي رجع عليهم بالتفضل والأنعام ليرجعوا إلى الطاعة والانقياد وفي الشرع ( الندم على معصية من حيث هي معصية مع عزم أن لا يعود إليها إذا قدر عليها). فقولنا الندم لما سيأتي من الحديث وقولنا على معصية لأن الندم على فعل لا يكون معصية بل مباحاً أو طاعة لا يسمى توبة، وقولنا ( من حيث هي معصية لأن من ندم على شرب الخمر لما فيه من الصداع ونزف العقل والإخلال بالمال والعرض لم يكن تائباً شرعاً، وقولنا (مع عزم أن لا يعود إليها) يأتي. وقال في شرح المقاصد : الندم لخوف النار أو لطمع الجنة هل يكون توبة فيه تردد بناء على أنه ندم لكونه معصية أم لا، والحق أنه لا لأن معنى كونه معصية أنها تبعد من الجنة وتقرب من النار ولا يقرب الرجل منها إلا لهذين المعنيين، نعم خلوص التوبة أن يكون لوجه الله تعالى لا لطمع جنة ولا لخوف نار لكن التوبة من المعصية من حيث هي معصية يعم الكل والله أعلم.
Regarding repentance (al-tawbah), and it contains two research topics: the first on its reality. Linguistically, it is returning (al-ruju'). Allah said: 'Then He relented toward them that they might repent' (9:118), meaning He turned to them with favor and grace so they would return to obedience and submission. Legally, it is regret (nadam) for a sin (ma'siya) inasmuch as it is a sin, along with determination not to return to it if able. So our statement 'regret' is based on the hadith to come; and our statement 'for a sin' because regret over an act that is not a sin but rather permissible or obedient is not called repentance; and our statement 'inasmuch as it is a sin' because one who regrets drinking wine due to the headache, loss of reason (its lightness and frivolity), and loss of wealth and honor is not a repentant legally. And our statement 'with determination not to return to it' will follow. And it is said in Sharh al-Maqasid: Is regret out of fear of Hell or hope of Paradise considered repentance? There is hesitation based on whether it is regret because it is a sin or not. The truth is that it is not, because the meaning of its being a sin is that it distances from Paradise and brings near to Hell, and one is only drawn to it for these two meanings. Yes, pure repentance is to be for the sake of Allah, not for hope of Paradise nor fear of Hell. However, repentance from a sin inasmuch as it is a sin encompasses all. And Allah knows best.
Source pages p. 342

Commentary
This passage defines repentance (al-tawbah) both linguistically and legally. Linguistically, it means 'returning'. Legally, it is defined as regret for a sin inasmuch as it is a sin, accompanied by determination not to return to it if able. The commentary then breaks down each component: regret is necessary based on hadith; the object of regret must be a sin (not a permissible act or obedience); the regret must be because the act is a sin, not because of its worldly harmful consequences (like headache or financial loss). Furthermore, it addresses whether repentance motivated by fear of punishment or hope of reward is valid. The answer is that such repentance is not the purest form, but it is still considered repentance because the regret is due to the sinfulness of the act, which entails both distance from Paradise and nearness to Hell. Pure repentance is for Allah alone, but the general definition includes all cases.

Correction: The passage clarifies that it is valid because the regret is due to the sinfulness of the act, which inherently involves these motivations.

The Condition of Determination in Repentance: Objections and Answers
Objections and answers regarding determination as a condition of repentance

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why scholars include determination not to repeat a sin as a condition of repentance and how objections to this condition are resolved.

Core Idea
True regret for sin inherently includes the resolve not to return to it.

In defining repentance, scholars debated whether explicit determination ('azm) is a separate condition or implicit in regret. This passage presents objections to making 'azm a requirement and the responses that defend its necessity. You'll see how the hadith 'regret is repentance' is interpreted to include this resolve.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Begin by reading the Arabic passage and its English translation. Notice the structure: objection, answer, further objection, further response.
2
Identify the first objection
The first objection argues that a person may not think about future sin, so determination is not always present. Pause to consider why someone might object to requiring 'azm.
3
Understand the initial response
The response uses the hadith 'regret is repentance' to argue that regret itself implies determination. Think about how regret for a past sin naturally leads to resolve not to repeat it.
4
Analyze the second objection and answer
The second objection says regret does not guarantee determination because one could regret but still desire the sin now. The answer distinguishes between incomplete regret and complete regret that necessarily includes resolve.
5
Synthesize the final conclusion
Combine the responses: regret for sin as sin necessarily entails determination. This shows why scholars accept 'azm as a condition of repentance.
The key is that true repentance (tawba) requires turning away from sin with resolve.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : (مع عزم أن لا يعود إليها) اعترض عليه بأن فعل المعصية في المستقبل قد لا يخطر بالبال لذهول ونحوه فالعزم على ترك المعاودة إنما يقارن التوبة في بعض الأحوال ولا تطرد في كل حال . وجوابه يفهم من قول الشارح ورد بأن الندم إلخ . مع عزم أن لا يعود إليها زيادة تقرير لما ذكر أولاً، وذلك لأن النادم على الأمر لا يكون إلا كذلك ولذلك ورد في الحديث الندم توبة. واعترض عليه بأن النادم على فعل في الماضي قد يريده في الحال أو الاستقبال فهذا القيد احتراز عنه وما ورد في الحديث محمول على الندم الكامل وهو أن يكون مع العزم على عدم العود أبداً. ورد بأن الندم على المعصية من حيث هي معصية يستلزم ذلك العزم كما لا يخفى.
His statement: 'with determination not to return to it' – it was objected to by saying that future commission of the sin may not occur to the mind due to heedlessness and the like, so determination to avoid recurrence only accompanies repentance in some cases and is not constant in every case. Its answer is understood from the commentator's statement: it was responded that regret implies that. The phrase 'with determination not to return to it' is additional confirmation of what was mentioned first, because the one who regrets something is only in that state. Hence the hadith 'regret is repentance.' And it was objected: the one who regrets a past act may desire it in the present or future, so this condition guards against that, and the hadith is interpreted as referring to complete regret, which includes determination never to return. And it was responded: regret for a sin inasmuch as it is a sin necessarily entails that determination, as is clear.
Source pages p. 342

Commentary
This passage addresses objections to including 'determination not to return' as a condition of repentance. First objection: a person might not think about future sin due to forgetfulness, so determination is not always present. Answer: the commentator says that regret itself implies determination; the hadith 'regret is repentance' supports that. Second objection: someone might regret a past sin but still desire it now or in the future. The condition guards against this. The hadith is taken to mean complete regret, which includes determination. The final response: regret for sin as sin necessarily entails determination because true regret for the disobedience motivates a resolve not to repeat it.

The Condition of Ability in Repentance and the Case of the Eunuch
Discusses the condition of ability in repentance and the eunuch's case with al-Amidi's support

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why repentance remains valid even if one loses the ability to commit the sin, and how the eunuch case illustrates this principle.

Core Idea
Repentance requires intention, not actual ability to sin again.

This passage tackles a subtle question: if someone loses the ability to commit a sin (e.g., a eunuch), can their repentance be valid? The author argues that the determination to abandon sin is meaningful even when ability is lost, as long as the intention is sincere. He supports this with al-Amidi's consensus-based argument. This lesson unpacks the reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic carefully, then the translation. Notice the key claim at the beginning and the investigation that follows.
The passage is dense; read slowly to grasp the grammatical nuances.
2
Identify the main positions
Two views are presented: the author's initial statement and al-Amidi's support. Distinguish between the two and note the grammatical debate.
Look for the phrases 'وفيه بحث' and 'ويؤيد ما قررناه'.
3
Understand the grammatical nuance
Focus on the attachment of 'if able' (إذا قدر) to either the determination or the negation. This affects the meaning and the validity of the eunuch's repentance.
The investigation (بحث) revolves around this grammatical point.
4
Apply the principle to the eunuch case
Connect the grammatical analysis to the ruling that the eunuch's repentance is valid by consensus, contrary to Abu Hashim.
Al-Amidi's quote is key: the determination is based on hypothetical ability.
5
Reflect on the importance of intention
Ask yourself why intention matters more than ability in repentance. This principle has broader implications for understanding sincere repentance.
This lesson's core idea: repentance is about the heart, not physical capability.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقولنا : إذا قدر لأن من سلب القدرة على الزنا وانقطع طمعه عن عود القدرة إليه إذا عزم على تركه لم يكن ذلك توبة منه وفيه بحث لأن قوله : إذا قدر ظرف لترك الفعل المستفاد من قوله : لا قوله : لا يعود وإنما قيد به لأن العزم على ترك الفعل في وقت إنما يتصور ممن قدر على ذلك الفعل وتركه في ذلك الوقت، ففائدة هذا القيد أن العزم على الترك ليس مطلقاً يتصور ممن سلب قدرته وانقطع طمعه بل هو مقيد بكونه على تقدير فرض القدرة وثبوتها فيتصور ذلك العزم من المسلوب أيضاً. ويؤيد ما قررناه قول الآمدي حيث قال : وإنما قلنا : عند كونه أهلاً لفعله في المستقبل احترازاً عما إذا زنى ثم جب، أو كان مشرفاً على الموت فإن العزم على ترك الفعل في المستقبل غير متصور منه لعدم تصور الفعل منه لعدم تصور الفعل منه ومع ذلك فإنه إذا ندم على ما فعل صحت توبته بإجماع السلف وقال أبو هاشم الزاني : إذا جب لا تصح توبته لأنه عاجز عنه وهو باطل بما إذا تاب عن الزنا وغيره وهو في مرض مخيف فإن توبته صحيحة بالإجماع، وإن كان جاز ما يعجزه عن الفعل في المستقبل هذه عبارته وأيضاً فقول المصنف : لم يكن ذلك توبة منه يدل على أنه مختار الكل أو الأكثر فينافيه ما صرح به من أن توبة المحبوب صحيحة عند غير أبي هاشم فتدبر. قوله : ( وفيه بحث لأن قوله إلخ مبنى كلام المصنف أن الظرف متعلق بالعزم ومبنى البحث على أنه متعلق بالنفي المستفاد من لا يعود ويجوز أن يتعلق بالعود أيضاً وهو في المآل كتعلقه بالنفي .
Our statement 'if able' is because one who has lost the ability to commit adultery and whose hope of regaining ability is cut off, if he resolves to abandon it, that is not repentance from him. And there is an investigation because his statement 'if able' is a condition for leaving the act derived from his statement 'not return' – it is only conditioned thus because the determination to leave the act at a certain time is only conceivable from one who has the ability for that act and its abandonment at that time. So the benefit of this condition is that the determination to abandon is not absolutely conceivable from one whose ability is stripped and whose hope is cut off; rather it is restricted to being upon the supposition of the existence and establishment of ability. Thus that determination is also conceivable from the disabled one. And what we have established is supported by the statement of al-Amidi: 'We only say “when he is capable of its future act” as a safeguard against the case where one fornicates then becomes a eunuch, or is near death; for determination to abandon the act in the future is not conceivable from him because the act is not conceivable from him. Yet if he regrets what he did, his repentance is valid by consensus of the Salaf.' Abu Hashim said: 'If the fornicator is made a eunuch, his repentance is not valid because he is incapable of it.' This is invalidated by the case where one repents from fornication while in a fear-inducing illness – his repentance is valid by consensus, even though the illness disables him from future acts. This is his wording. Also, the author's statement 'that is not repentance from him' indicates that he is the choice of all or most, contradicting what he stated that the repentance of the beloved is valid according to other than Abu Hashim. So reflect. His statement: 'And there is an investigation because his statement...' is based on the author's words that the adverbial phrase is attached to the determination; the basis of the investigation is that it is attached to the negation derived from 'he does not return'. It is also possible to attach it to the return, and in the end it is like its attachment to the negation.
Source pages p. 343

Commentary
This passage discusses the condition of ability (qudra) in repentance. It begins by stating that if a person loses the ability to commit a sin and has no hope of regaining it, his determination to abandon it is not considered true repentance because the determination is only meaningful when the act is possible. However, a counter-argument is presented: the condition 'if able' is actually a qualifier for the determination; it means that the determination should be made based on the hypothetical ability. So even a eunuch can still have the determination if we assume he had the ability. This is supported by al-Amidi, who states that the repentance of a eunuch is valid by consensus if he regrets his past act, despite his inability to repeat it. Abu Hashim's contrary opinion is rejected. The passage also clarifies the grammatical structure: the adverbial phrase 'if able' can be related either to the determination or to the prohibition ('not return').

Key Terms
القدرة
المجبوب
العزم
الآمدي

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: A person who is physically unable to commit a sin (e.g., a eunuch) cannot have valid repentance because they lack the ability to do the act again.
Correction: Repentance is based on sincere regret and intention to abandon sin, even if one is unable to commit the sin in the future. The determination is made on the hypothetical ability.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, why does al-Amidi consider the repentance of a eunuch valid?
A) He cannot sin again
B) He regrets and determination is based on hypothetical ability
C) He is forced to repent
D) Abu Hashim said so

Key Takeaway
Repentance is not invalidated by inability to repeat the sin; the intention to abandon, based on hypothetical ability, suffices.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

Two Rulings: Repentance of the Eunuch and the Terminally Ill
Presents two rulings on repentance: the eunuch and the terminally ill.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the two rulings on repentance for the eunuch and the terminally ill, and the reasoning behind them.

Core Idea
Repentance must be voluntary and sincerely intended, not coerced or based on impossibility.

In this lesson, we explore two specific cases related to repentance. The first concerns a eunuch who fornicated before castration: can his repentance be sincere if he physically cannot repeat the sin? The second involves someone who repents only when faced with a fear-inducing illness. Both cases test the principle that repentance requires genuine free choice. Let's examine what the scholars say.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice that the passage presents two distinct rulings.
2
Understand the eunuch ruling
Identify why Abu Hashim rejected the eunuch's repentance and why others accepted it. Focus on the concept of determination based on hypothetical ability.
The key term is 'تقدير القدرة' (supposition of ability).
3
Understand the terminally ill ruling
Understand how this ruling connects to the eunuch case. Why is repentance due to fear-inducing illness considered coerced and therefore not accepted?
The passage says 'ليس باختياره بل بإلجاء' (not voluntary but coerced).
4
Reflect on the principle
Ask yourself: what does this teach about the conditions of repentance? How does it relate to the broader Islamic concept of sincere tawbah?
Consider the balance between internal intention and external circumstances.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
البحث ( الثاني : في أحكامها الأول : الزاني المجبوب ( أي الذي زني ثم جب ( إذا ندم على الزنا وعزم أن لا يعود إليه على تقدير القدرة فهل يكون ذلك توبة منه منعه أبو هاشم، وزعم أنه لا يتحقق منه حقيقة العزم على عدم الفعل في المستقبل. إذ لا قدرة له على الفعل فيه ) وقال به الآخرون ( بناء على أنه يكفي لتلك الحقيقة تقدير القدرة ( والمأخذ ) في هذين القولين ( وأوضح ) كما ذكرناه ( الثاني : ) من الأحكام ( إن قلنا : لا يقبل ندم المجبوب ( فمن تاب ) عن معصية لمرض مخيف فهل يقبل ذلك منه ( لوجود التوبة أم لا ) لم يقبل ( لأنه ليس باختياره ) بل بإلجاء
The second research: on its rulings. First: The eunuch fornicator – i.e., one who fornicated then was castrated – if he regrets the fornication and determines not to return to it upon the supposition of ability, is that considered repentance from him? Abu Hashim prevented it, claiming that the reality of determination not to perform the act in the future is not realized from him, since he has no ability for the act. Others affirmed it, based on the sufficiency of supposition of ability for that reality. The basis of these two opinions is as we clarified. Second: If we say that the repentance of the eunuch is not accepted, then one who repents from a sin due to a fear-inducing illness – is that accepted from him for the existence of repentance or not? It is not accepted because it is not voluntary but coerced.
Source pages p. 343

Commentary
This lesson presents the two rulings within the second research on repentance. The first ruling concerns the eunuch fornicator: Abu Hashim denied the validity of his repentance because he cannot truly determine to abstain in the future due to his incapacity, while others accept it based on the principle that intention based on hypothetical ability suffices. The second ruling addresses the case of one who repents when terminally ill: if we already deny the eunuch's repentance due to lack of voluntary choice, then similarly repentance under duress (fear of death) is not accepted because it is coerced, not freely chosen.

التوبة

Checkpoint

A) It is accepted if he regrets

C) It depends on his sincerity
D) It is accepted only if he is cured

Key Takeaway
The validity of repentance depends on genuine voluntary choice and sincere intention, not external compulsion or impossibility of sin.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

The Definition of Iman as Tasdiq with Two Objections
Sanusi defines iman linguistically as tasdiq and in shari'a as tasdiq of the Prophet, with two objections and answers.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Ash'ari definition of iman as affirmation (tasdiq) of the Prophet and how it is defended against two objections.

Core Idea
Iman is inner affirmation of the Prophet's message, not mere words.

This passage from Sanusi lays out the foundational Ash'ari understanding of faith. It begins by establishing the linguistic meaning of iman as tasdiq, then defines it legally as a specific affirmation of the Prophet. Two potential objections are raised and answered, clarifying that Adam was a believer and that 'messenger' includes prophets.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis, then read the English translation. Let the author's own words set the stage.
2
Identify linguistic and legal definitions
Notice how Sanusi first gives the linguistic definition of iman as absolute tasdiq, then narrows it to a specific tasdiq of the Prophet in the legal sense.
The legal definition includes affirming matters known necessarily from the Prophet.
3
Examine the first objection and answer
The first objection claims that if iman is tasdiq of the Prophet, then Adam would not be a believer. Read Sanusi's answer carefully.
The answer relies on Adam being a messenger himself and believing in future messengers.
4
Examine the second objection and answer
The second objection notes that the hadith mentions only messengers, not prophets. See how Sanusi answers by expanding the meaning of 'messenger'.
Alternatively, prophets are followers of messengers, so affirming messengers implies affirming prophets.
5
Reflect on the core concept
Consider why the Ash'aris emphasize tasdiq as the essence of iman. How does this differ from other definitions?
This guards against reducing faith to mere outward actions.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
اعلم أن الإيمان في اللغة هو التصديق مطلقاً. قال تعالى حكاية عن إخوة يوسف {وما أنت بمؤمن لنا} أي بمصدق فيما حدثناك به. وقال عليه الصلاة والسلام: 'الإيمان أن تؤمن بالله وملائكته وكتبه ورسله' أي تصدق. ويقال: فلان يؤمن بكذا أي يصدقه ويعترف به. وأما في الشرع وهو متعلق ما ذكرنا من الأحكام فهو عندنا - يعني أتباع الشيخ أبي الحسن، وعليه أكثر الأئمة كالقاضي والأستاذ، ووافقهم على ذلك الصالحي وابن الراوندي من المعتزلة - التصديق للرسول فيما علم مجيئه به ضرورة فتفصيلاً فيما علم تفصيلاً وإجمالاً فيما علم إجمالاً. فهو في الشرع تصديق خاص.
قوله: (وقال: الإيمان أن تؤمن بالله وملائكته) الحديث: الإيمان الشرعي أن تصدق جزماً بوجود الله تعالى وبالأمور الأربعة واليوم الآخر هو القيامة وصف به لتأخره عن أيام الدنيا، والمراد بالإيمان به التصديق بما فيه من البعث والحساب وغيرهما من الأمور التي أخبر عنها الشارع. وهاهنا بحثان:
الأول: إن الإيمان لو كان عبارة عن التصديق بما ذكر لزم أن لا يكون آدم عليه السلام مؤمناً، لأنه لم يؤمن بالرسل لعدمهم قبله في زمانه. وجوابه بعد تسليم أن التصديق بالرسل داخل في إيمانه أيضاً أن نفسه كان رسولاً وآمن بأنه رسول ولا يلزم اتحاد المؤمن والمؤمن به لأن المؤمن نفسه والمؤمن به كونه رسولاً من عند الله تعالى، وأنه كان مؤمناً بأن الله تعالى سيوجد من ظهره رسلاً.
الثاني: إن الرسول أخص من النبي كما مر، فزوال الكتاب فليس في الحديث ما يدل على وجوب الإيمان بالأنبياء مع وجوبه. وجوابه أن المراد بالرسول هاهنا القدر المشترك بين الرسول والنبي، وهذا المرسل من عند الله تعالى لدعوة عباده سواء كان صاحب شريعة أم لا. وقد يجاب بأن الأنبياء تبع الرسل فالإيمان بهم إيمان بالأنبياء.
Know that faith (al-iman) linguistically is affirmation (al-tasdiq) absolutely. Allah Almighty said, narrating from the brothers of Joseph: 'And you are not a believer (mu'min) in us' meaning an affirmer (musaddiq) of what we told you. And he, peace and blessings be upon him, said: 'Faith is that you believe (tu'min) in Allah, His angels, His books, and His messengers' meaning you affirm (tusaddiq). And it is said: So-and-so believes (yu'min) in such-and-such, meaning he affirms it and acknowledges it. As for in the Law (al-shar'), and it pertains to the rulings we mentioned, it is according to us – meaning the followers of Abu al-Hasan, and upon it are most imams like al-Qadi and al-Ustadh, and al-Salihi and Ibn al-Rawandi from the Mu'tazila agreed with them – the affirmation of the Messenger regarding what is known to have come from him necessarily, in detail for what is known in detail, and in summary for what is known in summary. So it is in the Law a specific affirmation. His saying: 'Faith is that you believe in Allah and His angels' the hadith: Legal faith (al-iman al-shar'i) is that you affirm with certainty the existence of Allah Almighty and the four matters, and the Last Day which is the Resurrection, described as such for its delay from the days of the world; and what is meant by believing in it is affirming what is in it of resurrection, reckoning, and other matters that the Lawgiver has informed of. And here are two investigations: The first: If faith were an expression for affirming what was mentioned, it would necessitate that Adam, peace be upon him, not be a believer because he did not believe in the messengers due to their absence before him in his time. Its answer, after conceding that affirmation of the messengers is included in his faith as well, is that he himself was a messenger and he believed that he was a messenger, and there is no necessary unity of the believer and the believed-in because the believer is his self and the believed-in is his being a messenger from Allah Almighty, and that he was a believer that Allah Almighty would create messengers from his descendants. The second: The messenger (rasul) is more specific than the prophet (nabi) as has passed, so the book is nullified? Thus there is nothing in the hadith indicating the obligation of faith in the prophets along with its obligation. Its answer is that what is meant by messenger here is the commonality between the messenger and the prophet, and this is the one sent from Allah Almighty to call His servants, whether he has a law or not. And it may be answered that the prophets are followers of the messengers, so faith in them is faith in the prophets.
Source pages p. 351

Commentary
This passage is a definition followed by two objections and their answers. The author begins by stating that iman (faith) linguistically means absolute tasdiq (affirmation). He supports this with Qur'anic usage (the verse about Joseph's brothers) and the prophetic hadith 'Iman is that you believe in Allah...' meaning tasdiq. He then gives the Ash'ari legal definition: iman is the affirmation of the Prophet concerning what is known to have come from him necessarily, i.e., matters of necessity known to every Muslim, and in detail for detailed matters, and in summary for summary matters. This is a specific affirmation, not absolute. The commentary then explains the hadith further, listing the foundational beliefs, and raises two investigations (bahthan). The first objection: If iman is tasdiq of the Prophet, then Adam could not be a believer because he did not have a prophet to affirm. The answer: Adam was himself a messenger (according to Islamic belief) and affirmed his own messengership; also, he believed that Allah would send messengers from his progeny, so he affirmatively believed in them. The second objection: The hadith mentions only messengers, not prophets, yet belief in all prophets is obligatory. The answer: The term 'rasul' here includes the common meaning of one sent from Allah, which encompasses both messengers and prophets. Alternatively, prophets are followers of messengers, so affirming messengers implies affirming prophets.

Key Terms
الإيمان
التصديق
المؤمن
الرسول
النبي

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think iman is merely verbal confession or outward actions without inner affirmation.
Correction: The Ash'aris emphasize that the core of iman is tasdiq (inner affirmation) of the Prophet's message, as stated in this passage.

Checkpoint
According to the Ash'ari definition in this passage, what is the core meaning of iman in the legal sense?
A) Verbal confession with the tongue
B) Affirmation (tasdiq) of the Messenger regarding what is known necessarily
C) Knowledge of Allah alone
D) Performance of all good deeds

Key Takeaway
The Ash'ari definition of iman is inner affirmation (tasdiq) of the Prophet's message, defended against objections about Adam's faith and the inclusion of prophets.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you say 'I believe in the Prophet,' do you truly affirm in your heart every detail of what he brought, even the matters you find difficult to understand or accept? If not, what is holding you back?
?
The objection about Adam not believing in messengers could be a subtle doubt in your own heart: Do you trust that Allah has given every community sufficient guidance, even those before the Prophet? How does this lesson strengthen your certainty?
?
How often do you reduce your faith to mere words or rituals without checking the state of your inner affirmation (tasdiq)? What would it take for you to actively cultivate that inner certainty every day?

Legal Iman Requires Voluntary Commitment
Sanusi recounts the view that iman is ma'rifa and distinguishes it from tasdiq, then refines the definition through a problem.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why legal iman requires voluntary commitment to the implications of affirmation, and why mere involuntary knowledge or affirmation is insufficient.

Core Idea
Legal iman is more than knowledge—it requires voluntary submission.

This passage from Sanusi's commentary presents a critical nuance in defining iman. It examines the claim that iman is ma'rifa (knowledge) and contrasts it with tasdiq (affirmation), ultimately showing that the key to legal iman is choice in committing to the implications of belief. The discussion uses a problem-and-response format to refine the definition.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once through, then read the literal translation. Notice how Sanusi builds his argument step by step.
Pay attention to the terms ma'rifa, tasdiq, ikhtiyar, and taslim.
2
Understand the distinction between ma'rifa and tasdiq
Identify the defining features of each: tasdiq is an acquired, voluntary act of the heart; ma'rifa is involuntary knowledge. Note that legal iman must be tasdiq.
This distinction is crucial for the argument.
3
Analyze the problem raised
Sanusi presents two scenarios: involuntary tasdiq with commitment, and voluntary tasdiq without commitment. Both are discussed to test the rule.
Think about why these scenarios challenge the simple identification of iman with tasdiq.
4
Grasp the resolution
The passage concludes that the essential element is choice (ikhtiyar) in committing to the implications of tasdiq, not the affirmation itself. This is taslim (submission).
This resolution shows that iman includes a distinct element of wilful commitment.
5
Reflect on the term taslim
Consider why some scholars consider taslim as something additional to tasdiq. How does this affect the definition of iman?

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله: (وقيل: هو المعرفة) قيل: في الفرق بين المعرفة والتصديق أن التصديق عبارة عن ربط القلب على ما علم من أخبار المخبر وهو أمر كسبي ثبت باختيار المصدق ولهذا يؤمر به ويثاب عليه بل يجعل رأس العبادات بخلاف المعرفة فإنها أمر يحصل بلا كسب كمن وقع بصره على جسم فحصل له معرفة أنه جدار أو حجر، والإيمان الشرعي يجب أن يكون من الأول فإن النبي عليه السلام إذا ادعى النبوة وأظهر المعجزة فوقع صدقه في قلب أحد ضرورة من غير أن ينسب إليه اختيار لا يقال له في اللغة أنه صدقه فلا يكون إيماناً شرعياً كذا في شرح المقاصد، وفيه بحث فإن من حصل له تصديق بلا اختيار إذا التزم العمل بموجبه يكون إيماناً اتفاقاً ولو صدق النبي عليه السلام بالنظر إلى معجزاته اختياراً ولم يلتزم عمل بموجبه بل عانده فهو كافر اتفاقاً، فعلم أن المعتبر في الإيمان الشرعي هو اختيار في التزام موجب التصديق لا في نفسه وهذا هو التسليم الذي اعتبره بعض الفضلاء أمراً زائداً على التصديق فليتأمل.
His saying: (And it was said: it is knowledge (al-ma'rifa)). It was said: The difference between knowledge (ma'rifa) and affirmation (tasdiq) is that affirmation is an expression for the heart's binding to what is known from the reports of the informant, and it is an acquired matter established by the choice of the affirmer, and therefore one is commanded to do it and rewarded for it, and it is made the head of acts of worship, unlike knowledge (ma'rifa), for it is a matter that occurs without acquisition, like one whose sight falls on an object and he acquires the knowledge that it is a wall or a stone. And legal faith (al-iman al-shar'i) must be of the first type, because if the Prophet, peace and blessings be upon him, claims prophethood and shows a miracle, and his truthfulness occurs in someone's heart necessarily without any choice attributed to him, it is not said in language that he affirmed him, so it would not be legal faith. Thus in Sharh al-Maqasid. But there is investigation in this: for if someone acquires an affirmation without choice but then commits to acting in accordance with it, it is faith by agreement; and if he affirms the Prophet, peace and blessings be upon him, by looking at his miracles with choice but does not commit to acting in accordance with it and rather opposes him, he is a disbeliever by agreement. So it is known that what counts in legal faith is choice in committing to the implications of affirmation, not in the affirmation itself. And this is the submission (al-taslim) that some virtuous people consider as something additional to affirmation. So let one reflect.
Source pages p. 352

Commentary
This passage presents an objection-and-answer style discussion. It begins by reporting the opinion that iman is ma'rifa (knowledge), attributed to Jahm ibn Safwan. The commentary then explains the difference between ma'rifa and tasdiq (affirmation). Tasdiq is defined as a binding of the heart based on reports, an acquired (kasbi) act that earns reward. Ma'rifa, by contrast, is involuntary knowledge, like knowing a wall is a wall when you see it. Legal iman must be the first type (voluntary tasdiq), because if someone is compelled to believe by a miracle without choice, it is not called tasdiq in language and thus not legal iman. However, a problem is raised: if someone has involuntary tasdiq but still commits to its implications, it is iman by agreement; and if someone voluntarily affirms but does not commit and opposes, he is a disbeliever by agreement. Thus, the crucial element in legal iman is choice (ikhtiyar) regarding commitment to the implications of tasdiq, not the tasdiq itself. This choice is called taslim (submission), considered an additional element beyond mere tasdiq by some scholars. The author ends with a call for reflection, indicating a nuanced position.

Key Terms
المعرفة
التصديق
الكسب
الاختيار
التسليم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Any inner belief or knowledge, even if involuntary, constitutes iman.
Correction: Legal iman requires voluntary choice in committing to the implications of belief; mere involuntary conviction is not sufficient.

Checkpoint
What does the passage identify as the key element in legal iman (al-iman al-shar'i)?
A) Involuntary knowledge (ma'rifa)
B) Voluntary affirmation (tasdiq) alone
C) Choice (ikhtiyar) in committing to the implications of tasdiq
D) Performing prayers

Key Takeaway
Legal iman is defined as voluntary commitment to the implications of affirmation, not merely passive knowledge or affirmation.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You know that Allah is true and Muhammad is His Messenger—but is that knowledge passive (ma'rifa) or active with commitment? What specific area of your life today still contradicts what you claim to affirm, and what step will you take to close that gap?
?
The lesson says that even involuntary affirmation followed by commitment counts as iman. But you have been given the choice. Will you continue to coast on inherited belief, or will you today consciously renew your taslim—your submission to the full implications of faith? What is holding you back from that deliberate act?
?
Imagine standing before Allah with a heart that affirmed the truth but never committed to living it. The passage calls that kufr by agreement. Does that description unsettle you? What is one concrete change you will make in the next 24 hours to prove that your iman is not mere tasdiq but submission?

The Karramiyya View: Iman is Merely the Shahada
Sanusi presents and critiques the Karramiyya definition of iman.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Karramiyya definition of iman and the logical contradictions it generates.

Core Idea
The Karramiyya reduce iman to a verbal formula, creating paradoxes about salvation.

Sanusi presents the controversial view of the Karramiyya, who define iman solely as uttering the two shahadas, divorced from inner belief. This leads to peculiar consequences that Sanusi critiques. In this lesson, we examine their position and the attempted reconciliations.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Notice the Karramiyya's definition and its implications.
Pay attention to the two different reports from Sharh al-Maqasid and Tafsir al-Qadi.
2
Identify the paradox
The passage says a person who hides disbelief but professes the shahada is a believer yet deserves eternal fire. Reflect on why this is contradictory.
Consider the purpose of iman: entry into Paradise.
3
Consider the attempted reconciliation
The author mentions a reconciliation between the two reports: one refers to minimal saving faith, the other to faith in an absolute sense. But Sanusi rejects this reconciliation as incoherent.
Ask: Why does Sanusi find the reconciliation unacceptable?

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله: (وقالت الكرامية هو كلمتا الشهادة) ولا يشترطون التصديق والمعرفة حتى إن من أضمر الكفر وأظهر الإيمان يكون مؤمناً إلا أنه يستحق الخلود في النار، ومن أضمر الإيمان ولم يتفق منه الإظهار والإقرار لم يستحق الجنة كذا في شرح المقاصد، والمذكور في تفسير القاضي أن مذهب الكرامية أن الإيمان مجرد كلمة الشهادة إذا خلا قلبه من الاعتقاد حتى لو اعتقد خلافه لم يكن مؤمناً، وقد تلفق بينهما بأن ما ذكره القاضي هو الإيمان المنجي من النار، والمذكور في شرح المقاصد هو الإيمان مطلقاً، وأنت خبير بأن نتيجة الإيمان هو الدخول في الجنة ونتيجة الكفر هو الخلود في النار فالقول بإيمانه مع الخلود في النار كما ذكره في شرح المقاصد مما لا وجه له هذا وقد يجعل الإيمان اسماً للإقرار بحقية ما جاء به الرسول عليه السلام ويشترط معه معرفة القلب حتى لا يكون الإقرار بدونها إيماناً وإليه ذهب الرقاشي زاعماً أن المعرفة ضرورية فلا يجعل من الإيمان لكونه اسماً لفعل مكتسب لا ضروري وقد يشترط التصديق وإليه ذهب بأن الإقرار الخالي عن التصديق لا يكون إيماناً وعند اقترانه به يكون الإيمان هو القطان وصرح الإقرار فقط. • قوله : ( وقال قوم أنه أعمال الجوارح فذهب الخوارج إلخ ) المفهوم من شرح المقاصد أن الإيمان عند هؤلاء اسم لفعل القلب واللسان والجوارح جميعاً حيث قال : وأما على الرابع وهو أن
His saying: (And the Karramiyya said: it is the two words of testimony (kalimata al-shahada)). They do not require affirmation (tasdiq) or knowledge (ma'rifa); even if someone conceals disbelief and manifests faith, he is a believer, except that he deserves eternal punishment in the Fire; and if someone conceals faith and no manifestation and confession occurs from him, he does not deserve Paradise. Thus in Sharh al-Maqasid. And what is mentioned in Tafsir al-Qadi is that the school of the Karramiyya is that faith is merely the word of testimony if his heart is empty of belief, so that even if he believes the opposite, he is not a believer. And reconciliation between them is made by saying that what al-Qadi mentioned is the faith that saves from the Fire, and what is mentioned in Sharh al-Maqasid is faith absolutely. And you are aware that the result of faith is entry into Paradise and the result of disbelief is eternal punishment in the Fire, so saying that he is a believer while still in the Fire forever, as mentioned in Sharh al-Maqasid, has no justification. And faith may be made a name for the confession (al-iqrar) of the truth of what the Messenger, peace be upon him, brought, and it is conditioned with knowledge of the heart so that confession without it is not faith. This was the view of al-Raqqashi, claiming that knowledge is necessary and thus not made part of faith because it is a name for an acquired act, not a necessary one. And some condition affirmation (tasdiq); this was the view that confession devoid of affirmation is not faith, and when accompanied by it, faith is... al-Qattan? and he explicitly stated that it is only confession. His saying: (And some said that it is the actions of the limbs, so the Khawarij went... etc.) What is understood from Sharh al-Maqasid is that faith according to these people is a name for the action of the heart, tongue, and limbs all together, where he said: As for the fourth, which is that...
Source pages p. 352

Commentary
This passage enumerates and critiques several theological schools on the nature of iman. It begins with the Karramiyya view that iman is merely uttering the two shahadas (kalimatay al-shahada) without requiring inner affirmation or knowledge. This leads to paradoxes: a person who inwardly disbelieves but outwardly professes is considered a believer yet deserves eternal hell; a person with inner faith but no outward profession does not deserve paradise. The author notes a reconciliation: one interpretation (from al-Qadi) says the Karramiyya mean by iman the minimal faith that saves from eternal punishment, while another (from Sharh al-Maqasid) is an absolute definition. The author criticizes the latter because if the result of iman is paradise, then someone who is a believer but destined for hell is incoherent. Next, the passage mentions the view of al-Raqqashi: iman is verbal confession (iqrar) conditioned on heart knowledge, but al-Raqqashi insists that knowledge is not part of iman because iman is an acquired act (kasbi), not necessary. Then a view that conditions tasdiq (affirmation) is mentioned, referencing al-Qattan? who said iman is only confession. Finally, the passage begins to introduce the view that iman is actions (a'mal al-jawarih), attributed to the Khawarij and some Mu'tazila, but the text cuts off. The commentary notes that according to Sharh al-Maqasid, these groups consider iman to encompass heart, tongue, and limbs.

Key Terms
كرامية
كلمتا الشهادة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The Karramiyya view that iman is only verbal profession is consistent.
Correction: Sanusi shows it leads to contradictions, as someone with inner disbelief is considered a believer but still destined for eternal fire, making the definition incoherent.

Checkpoint
According to the Karramiyya as described in this passage, what happens to one who conceals disbelief but professes the two shahadas?
A) He is a disbeliever and will enter Hell
B) He is a believer but deserves eternal punishment in Hell
C) He is a believer and will enter Paradise
D) He is a hypocrite and will be punished temporarily

Key Takeaway
The Karramiyya definition of iman as mere verbal profession leads to paradoxical consequences that Sanusi rejects.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you recite the shahada, do you ever stop to check whether your heart truly affirms what your tongue says? If your iman were tested by your inner state alone, what would be the verdict?
?
Sanusi shows that even the Karramiyya admit that outward profession without inner belief leads to Hell. Is there any area of your life where you rely on outward rituals while your heart is disconnected?
?
If you knew that a single genuine profession of faith could save you, but that without inner conviction it is worthless, what would you change about how you approach your daily declaration of iman?

Other Definitions: Confession, Affirmation, and Actions
Sanusi presents three further opinions on the definition of iman.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the alternative views of al-Raqqashi, those who condition tasdiq, and the view that iman is actions.

Core Idea
Iman has been defined as verbal confession conditioned on knowledge, or as affirmation, or as all actions of heart, tongue, and limbs.

After discussing the Karramiyya, Sanusi turns to other theological positions on the nature of iman. These include al-Raqqashi's condition of knowledge, the requirement of tasdiq, and the comprehensive view that iman encompasses all good deeds. This lesson examines these opinions as presented in the source.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Identify each distinct view: al-Raqqashi, those who condition tasdiq, and the action-based view.
Notice that the text cuts off at the end when introducing the action view.
2
Compare the three views
For each view, note what is required for iman (iqrar, ma'rifa, tasdiq, actions) and whether any element is excluded. See how they differ from the Karramiyya.
Al-Raqqashi excludes knowledge from iman because it is necessary, not acquired.
3
Reflect on the cut-off
The passage ends mid-sentence while introducing the fourth view that iman is actions of heart, tongue, and limbs. Consider what additional information might follow in the original text.
This view is attributed to the Khawarij and some Mu'tazila.

His saying: (And the Karramiyya said: it is the two words of testimony (kalimata al-shahada)). They do not require affirmation (tasdiq) or knowledge (ma'rifa); even if someone conceals disbelief and manifests faith, he is a believer, except that he deserves eternal punishment in the Fire; and if someone conceals faith and no manifestation and confession occurs from him, he does not deserve Paradise. Thus in Sharh al-Maqasid. And what is mentioned in Tafsir al-Qadi is that the school of the Karramiyya is that faith is merely the word of testimony if his heart is empty of belief, so that even if he believes the opposite, he is not a believer. And reconciliation between them is made by saying that what al-Qadi mentioned is the faith that saves from the Fire, and what is mentioned in Sharh al-Maqasid is faith absolutely. And you are aware that the result of faith is entry into Paradise and the result of disbelief is eternal punishment in the Fire, so saying that he is a believer while still in the Fire forever, as mentioned in Sharh al-Maqasid, has no justification. And faith may be made a name for the confession (al-iqrar) of the truth of what the Messenger, peace be upon him, brought, and it is conditioned with knowledge of the heart so that confession without it is not faith. Al-Rawandi's Definition of Faith as Tasdiq
Al-Rawandi defines iman as tasdiq with three modes.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand al-Rawandi's definition of iman as a specific tasdiq that varies in detail according to the clarity of knowledge, and why this matters for identifying disbelief.

Core Idea
Faith is affirming the messenger in proportion to your knowledge.

Al-Rawandi, a Mu'tazilite scholar, offers a precise definition of faith that ties it directly to knowledge. He distinguishes between matters known necessarily and those known in detail or summary. This passage not only defines iman but also raises the stakes: failing to affirm what is obviously part of religion makes one a disbeliever.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic passage once and then its translation. Notice how al-Rawandi's definition is compact and technical.
The passage is from a commentary, so it includes explanation.
2
Identify the three modes of tasdiq
Look for the words تفصيلاً and إجمالاً. The passage mentions tasdiq in detail, in summary, and necessarily. Understand how these correspond to knowledge levels.
Necessarily known matters are those known without proof.
3
Reflect on the practical implication
The commentary gives examples and states that denying a necessary matter when asked results in disbelief. Think about why this is important for defining the boundaries of faith.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الراوندي من المعتزلة ( التصديق للرسول فيما علم مجيئه به ضرورة فتفصيلا فيما علم تفصيلاً وإجمالاً فيما علم إجمالاً ) فهو في الشرع تصديق خاص. قوله : فيما علم مجيئه به ضرورة أي فيما اشتهر كونه من الدين بحيث يعلمه العامة بلا دليل كوحدة الصانع ووجوب الصلاة وحرمة الخمر حتى لو لم يصدق بوجوب الصلاة مثلاً عند السؤال عنها كان كافرا عند الجمهور.
Al-Rawandi from the Mu'tazila: (Tasdiq of the messenger regarding what one knows that he came with necessarily, then in detail regarding what is known in detail, and in summary regarding what is known in summary). So it is in the Sacred Law a specific tasdiq. His statement: 'regarding what one knows that he came with necessarily' means regarding what is well-known as part of the religion such that the common people know it without proof, like the oneness of the Creator, the obligation of prayer, and the prohibition of wine, such that if one does not affirm the obligation of prayer, for example, when asked about it, he is a disbeliever according to the majority.
Source pages p. 352

Commentary
This passage presents and explains the definition of iman according to al-Rawandi of the Mu'tazila. The definition states that iman is the affirmation (tasdiq) of the messenger concerning matters known to have come from him. Matters known necessarily are affirmed in a general way, while detailed matters require detailed affirmation. The commentary clarifies that 'necessarily' refers to matters so well-known that even the common people know them without needing proof, such as tawhid, prayer, and wine prohibition. Failure to affirm such a matter when asked makes one a disbeliever by consensus, showing that iman involves specific tasdiq corresponding to the clarity of the message.

Key Terms
تصديق
إيمان
ضرورة
تفصيل
إجمال

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that tasdiq must be explicit for every detail, even for basic, widely known doctrines.
Correction: The passage shows that for necessarily known matters, a general affirmation (ijmāl) suffices. Detailed affirmation is required only for details that are known in detail.

Checkpoint

A) He is still a believer because he might affirm it in general.
B) He is a disbeliever according to the majority.
C) He is excused because he might not know.
D) He is considered a sinner but not a disbeliever.

Key Takeaway
Faith according to al-Rawandi is a specific affirmation of the messenger, varying in detail with knowledge, and failure to affirm necessarily known matters is disbelief.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

If you were asked right now to articulate why prayer is obligatory, would your response reflect the tasdiq of one who has thought it through or one who merely assumes it? This lesson forces you to examine not just what you believe but how you believe it.
?

Have you ever found yourself hesitant or dismissive when questioned about the prohibition of wine or the unity of God? If so, what does that say about the quality of your tasdiq? Are you willing to address that gap today?
?
Your faith is not a checkbox—it has modes and depths. According to this passage, your tasdiq must match your knowledge. What areas of necessary knowledge do you know only superficially? Will you commit to deepening your affirmation from general to detailed, starting with one matter you have taken for granted?
