Jahm's Definition of Iman as Ma'rifa and the Role of Choice
Jahm defines iman as ma'rifa; commentary distinguishes ma'rifa from tasdiq and debates choice.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the difference between ma'rifa and tasdiq, and why choice in commitment is essential for legal iman.

Core Idea
Legal iman requires voluntary commitment, not just knowledge or affirmation.

Jahm bin Safwan defined iman as mere knowledge of God, but the scholars distinguish this from tasdiq (affirmation), which is voluntary. This passage explores that distinction and argues that the key element in legal iman is not the affirmation itself but the choice to act upon it, known as taslim (submission).

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
First, read the Arabic passage and its literal translation to understand the argument's flow.
2
Identify the distinction between ma'rifa and tasdiq
Notice how ma'rifa is involuntary (like seeing a wall) while tasdiq is an acquired act of the heart involving choice.
This sets up the problem.
3
Understand the resolution about ikhtiyar in iltizam
The author resolves that legal iman requires choice in committing to the implications of tasdiq, not in the tasdiq itself. This is called taslim.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقيل: الإيمان هو المعرفة تقوم بالله وهو مذهب جهم بن صفوان وقوم بالله وبما جاءت به الرسل إجمالاً وهو منقول عن بعض الفقهاء. قوله: (وقيل: هو المعرفة قيل : في الفرق بين المعرفة والتصديق أن التصديق عبارة عن ربط القلب على ما علم من أخبار المخبر وهو أمر كسبي ثبت باختيار المصدق ولهذا يؤمر به ويثاب عليه بل يجعل رأس العبادات بخلاف المعرفة فانها[؟] بما يحصل بلا كسب كمن وقع بصره على جسم فحصل له معرفة أنه جدار أو حجر، والإيمان الشرعي يجب أن يكون من الأول فإن النبي عليه السلام إذا ادعى النبوة وأظهر المعجزة فوقع صدقه في قلب أحد ضرورة من غير أن ينسب إليه اختيار لا يقال له في اللغة أنه صدقه فلا يكون إيمانا شرعياً كذا في شرح المقاصد، وفيه بحث فإن من حصل له تصديق بلا اختيار إذا التزم العمل بموجبه يكون إيماناً اتفاقاً ولو صدق النبي عليه السلام بالنظري معجزاته اختيارا ولم يلتزم عمل بموجبه بل عانده فهو كافر اتفاقاً، فعلم أن المعتبر في الإيمان الشرعي هو اختيار في التزام موجب التصديق لا في نفسه وهذا هو التسليم الذي اعتبره بعض الفضلاء أمرا زائداً على التصديق فليتأمل.
And it was said: iman is knowledge (ma'rifa) established in God—that is the madhhab of Jahm bin Safwan and some people—[knowledge] of God and of what the messengers brought, in summary, and it is transmitted from some jurists. His statement: 'And it was said: it is ma'rifa.' It was said: The difference between ma'rifa and tasdiq is that tasdiq is an expression of the heart's binding to what is known from the reports of the informant, and it is an acquired matter established by the choice of the one who affirms, and for this reason one is commanded to do it and rewarded for it, indeed it is made the head of acts of worship, unlike ma'rifa, for it [?] occurs without acquisition, like when one's sight falls upon an object and he obtains knowledge that it is a wall or a stone. And iman in the Sacred Law must be of the first type [i.e., tasdiq], because if the Prophet, peace be upon him, claims prophethood and shows a miracle, and his truthfulness occurs in someone's heart necessarily, without any choice attributed to him, it is not said in language that he affirmed him, so it is not iman legally—this is stated in Sharh al-Maqasid. But there is discussion: if a person obtains tasdiq without choice but then commits to acting in accordance with it, he is a believer by agreement. And if someone affirms the Prophet with his intellectual miracles by choice but does not commit to acting accordingly, rather opposes him, he is a disbeliever by agreement. Thus it is known that what is considered in legal iman is choice (ikhtiyar) in committing to the implications of tasdiq, not in the tasdiq itself. This is the 'submission' (taslim) that some scholars consider an additional element beyond tasdiq. So let it be pondered.
Source pages p. 352

Commentary
This passage presents the view that iman is ma'rifa (knowledge), attributed to Jahm bin Safwan, and then explains the difference between ma'rifa and tasdiq. Ma'rifa is involuntary, like seeing a wall, while tasdiq is an acquired act of the heart involving choice and reward. The author then discusses a problem: if tasdiq occurs without choice, it is not legally iman. However, if that involuntary tasdiq is followed by commitment to act, it becomes iman by agreement. Conversely, voluntary tasdiq without commitment is not iman. Hence, the essential element in legal iman is choice in accepting the implications of tasdiq, not the tasdiq itself. This additional element is called taslim (submission).

Key Terms
معرفة
تصديق
كسب
اختيار
التزام
تسليم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Mere knowledge (ma'rifa) of God is sufficient for iman.
Correction: Legal iman requires voluntary commitment (ikhtiyar fi al-iltizam) to the implications of tasdiq, not just knowledge.

Checkpoint
According to the discussion, what is the key element that constitutes legal iman?
A) Mere knowledge (ma'rifa) of God.
B) Voluntary tasdiq in the heart.
C) Choice (ikhtiyar) in committing to the implications of tasdiq.
D) Verbal confession alone.

Key Takeaway
Legal iman is defined by voluntary submission (taslim) to the implications of affirmation, not by mere knowledge or even involuntary affirmation.

If your heart involuntarily affirms the Prophet's truth, but your actions remain unchanged, would you be safe? Examine your daily choices: where does your commitment fall short of your belief?
?
The passage says faith hinges on a choice—your choice. Are you actively choosing to submit to the implications of your faith, or are you coasting on passive knowledge? What is one concrete step you will take today to prove your submission is real?

The Karramiyya Definition of Iman as Mere Utterance
Karramiyya definition and its critique

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Karramiyya definition of iman as mere verbal utterance of the shahada and the theological issues it raises.

Core Idea
Iman can be defined as only the shahada, but that leads to a troubling paradox.

The Karramiyya school defined iman in a minimal way – as simply uttering the two words of shahada, without requiring inner belief. This passage from Sanusi's commentary presents their view and then scrutinizes its implications, showing why such a definition is problematic. Let's see how they justified it and why the author finds it unsatisfactory.

Guided Path
1
Read the Karramiyya definition
Start by reading the passage where the Karramiyya state their definition. Notice that they explicitly reject the need for tasdiq or ma'rifa.
The definition is: 'iman is the two words of shahada.'
2
Understand the implications
Consider the two cases given: a person who inwardly disbelieves but outwardly declares iman (he is a believer but eternally punished), and one who inwardly believes but does not utter the shahada (he does not enter Paradise). This seems paradoxical.
This shows the practical consequences of the definition.
3
Note the discrepancy between sources
The passage mentions a difference between Tafsir al-Qadi and Sharh al-Maqasid. Identify what each source says.
Al-Qadi says mere utterance even if empty heart; Sharh al-Maqasid says utterance suffices but eternal punishment for hypocrites.
4
See the attempted reconciliation and critique
Read the author's attempted reconciliation and his critique. He finds it absurd that a 'believer' would be eternally punished.
The author says the result of iman is Paradise, not the Fire.
5
Consider alternative definitions
The passage ends with other views that require inner knowledge (ma'rifa) or tasdiq in addition to utterance. Reflect on why these are more coherent.
These views address the paradox by adding inner conditions.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقالت الكرامية : هو كلمتا الشهادة. قوله : ( وقالت الكرامية هو كلمتا الشهادة ولا يشترطون التصديق والمعرفة حتى إن من أضمر الكفر وأظهر الإيمان يكون مؤمناً إلا أنه يستحق الخلود في النار، ومن أضمر الإيمان ولم يتفق منه الإظهار والإقرار لم يستحق الجنة كذا في شرح المقاصد، والمذكور في تفسير القاضي أن مذهب الكرامية أن الإيمان مجرد كلمة الشهادة إذا خلا قلبه من الاعتقاد حتى لو اعتقد خلافه لم يكن مؤمناً، وقد تلفق بينهما بأن ما ذكره القاضي هو الإيمان المنجي من النار، والمذكور في شرح المقاصد هو الإيمان مطلقاً، وأنت خبير بأن نتيجة الإيمان هو الدخول في الجنة ونتيجة الكفر هو الخلود في النار فالقول بإيمانه مع الخلود في النار كما ذكره في شرح المقاصد مما لا وجه له هذا وقد يجعل الإيمان اسماً للإقرار بحقية ما جاء به الرسول عليه السلام ويشترط معه معرفة القلب حتى لا يكون الإقرار بدونها إيماناً وإليه ذهب الرقاشي زاعماً أن المعرفة ضرورية فلا يجعل من الإيمان لكونه اسما لفعل مكتسب لا ضروري وقد يشترط التصديق وإليه ذهب بأن الإقرار الخالي عن التصديق لا يكون إيماناً وعند اقترانه به يكون الإيمان هو القطان وصرح الإقرار فقط.
And the Karramiyya said: It is the two words of shahada. His statement: 'And the Karramiyya said it is the two words of shahada, and they do not require tasdiq or ma'rifa, such that if someone conceals disbelief and manifests iman, he is a believer, except that he deserves eternal punishment in the Fire; and if someone conceals iman but does not manifest and express it, he does not deserve Paradise.' This is as stated in Sharh al-Maqasid. And it is mentioned in Tafsir al-Qadi that the madhhab of the Karramiyya is that iman is merely the word of shahada even if his heart is empty of belief, even if he believes the opposite, he is not a believer. And reconciliation has been attempted: what al-Qadi mentioned is the iman that saves from the Fire, while what is mentioned in Sharh al-Maqasid is iman in an absolute sense. But you know that the result of iman is entry into Paradise and the result of disbelief is eternal punishment in the Fire, so the statement that one is a believer while being in the Fire eternally, as mentioned in Sharh al-Maqasid, has no basis. And iman may be used as a name for the acknowledgment (iqrar) of the truth of what the messenger brought, with heart knowledge also required, so that acknowledgment without it is not iman. This is the view of al-Raqqashi, who claimed that ma'rifa is necessary, but it is not considered iman because iman is a name for an acquired act, not a necessary one. And tasdiq may also be required; the one who went to this view said that acknowledgment devoid of tasdiq is not iman, and when they are combined, iman is ...
Source pages p. 352

Commentary
This passage presents the Karramiyya definition of iman as merely uttering the two words of shahada, without requiring inner belief or knowledge. The commentary explains that, according to this view, a person who inwardly disbelieves but outwardly declares iman is a believer, though he will be eternally punished. Conversely, someone who inwardly believes but does not utter the shahada will not enter Paradise. The author then notes a discrepancy between Tafsir al-Qadi and Sharh al-Maqasid, and an attempted reconciliation: al-Qadi's description (mere utterance) is the iman that saves from the Fire, while Sharh al-Maqasid describes absolute iman. However, the author finds this reconciliation problematic because the result of iman is Paradise, not eternal punishment. Finally, other views are mentioned that require knowledge or tasdiq in addition to verbal acknowledgment.

Correction: The passage shows they distinguish between saving iman and absolute iman, but the author finds even that distinction problematic because it still allows a 'believer' to be eternally punished.

Checkpoint

A) He is a hypocrite and not a believer.

C) He is a believer and will enter Paradise.
D) He is a disbeliever because inner disbelief overrides.

Key Takeaway
The Karramiyya definition of iman as mere utterance leads to the problematic conclusion that a hypocrite is a believer deserving eternal punishment, which the author finds untenable.

?

The Fourth View: Iman as Heart, Tongue, and Limbs
Passage presents the fourth view and its offshoots (Khawarij and Mu'tazila) with internal Mu'tazila subdivisions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the view that iman includes heart, tongue, and limbs leads to the Khawarij and Mu'tazila positions, and the internal disagreements within the Mu'tazila.

Core Idea
Iman that includes actions splits into Khawarij excommunication and Mu'tazila intermediate position.

This passage introduces the fourth major view: iman is the combined action of heart, tongue, and limbs. This raises a key question: what about the one who abandons action? The Khawarij and Mu'tazila gave opposite answers, and even the Mu'tazila disagreed among themselves on which actions constitute iman.

Guided Path
1
Read the fourth view definition
Read the passage that states iman is a name for the action of heart, tongue, and all limbs. Note the three components: acknowledgment (iqrar), tasdiq, and action.
This is the starting point for the discussion.
2
Identify the two main offshoots
Identify the two positions that follow from this view: Khawarij say the one who abandons action leaves iman and enters disbelief; Mu'tazila say he is in an intermediate position (al-manzila bayn al-manzilatayn).
Both groups agree that iman includes actions, but differ on the status of the sinner.
3
Examine the Mu'tazila internal disagreement
Note that the Mu'tazila themselves disagreed on what actions are required: Abu Ali and Abu Hashim said it is performing obligations and avoiding prohibitions; Abu al-Hudhayl and Abd al-Jabbar said it is all acts of obedience, even supererogatory, though they considered the extreme view of expelling a sinner for missing a supererogatory act irrational.
4
Reflect on the consequence
Reflect on the implication that defining iman as including all actions leads to different judgments on who is a believer.
This shows how a single definition can branch into multiple madhhabs.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : ( وقال قوم أنه أعمال الجوارح فذهب الخوارج إلخ ) المفهوم من شرح المقاصد أن الإيمان عند هؤلاء اسم لفعل القلب واللسان والجوارح جميعا حيث قال : وأما على الرابع وهو أن يكون الإيمان اسماً لفعل القلب واللسان والجوارح على ما يقال : أنه إقرار باللسان وتصديق بالجنان وعمل بالأركان فقد يجعل تارك العمل خارجا عن الإيمان داخلاً في الكفر، وإليه ذهب الخوارج أو غير داخل فـ ، وهو المنزلة بين المنزلتين وإليه ذهبت المعتزلة، إلا أنهم اختلفوا في الأعمال، فعند أبي علي وأبي هاشم فعل الواجبات وترك المحظورات وعند أبي الهذيل وعبد الجبار فعل الطاعات واجبة كانت أو مندوبة إلا أن الخروج عن الإيمان وحرمان دخول الجنة بترك المندوب مما لا ينبغي أن يكون مذهباً لعاقل، إلى هناك كلامه . بأسرها ( فرضاً ) كانت ( أو نفلاً، وذهب الجبائي وابنه وأكثر المعتزلة البصرية إلى أنه الطاعات المفترضة) من الأفعال والتروك ( دون النوافل، وقال السلف : ( أي بعضهم كابن مجاهد ( وأصحاب الأثر أي المحدوثون كلهم ( إنه مجموع هذه الثلاثة فهو ) عندهم ( تصديق بالجنان وإقرار باللسان وعمل بالأركان ووجه الضبط ) في هذه المذاهب الثمانية ( أن الإيمان لا يخرج بإجماع المسلمين ( عن فعل القلب و) فعل ( الجوارح، فهو ) حينئذ ( إما فعل القلب فقط وهو المعرفة ) على الوجهين، (أو التصديق) المذكور ( وإما فعل الجوارح فقط وهو إما اللسان) أي فعله (وهو الكلمتان أو غيره أي غير فعل اللسان ( وهو العمل بالطاعات ) المطلقة أو المفترضة ، ( وإما فعل القلب والجوارح معاً، والجارحة إما اللسان ) وحده (أو سائر الجوارح ) أي جميعها، فقد انضبط بهذا التقسيم المذاهب كلها
His statement: 'And some people said that it is the actions of the limbs, so the Khawarij and others went...' The understanding from Sharh al-Maqasid is that iman according to these people is a name for the action of the heart, tongue, and all limbs. He said: As for the fourth [view], which is that iman is a name for the action of the heart, tongue, and limbs, as it is said: it is acknowledgment by the tongue, tasdiq by the heart, and action by the pillars. Then the one who abandons action may be considered outside iman, entering into disbelief—that is the view of the Khawarij; or he may not be considered outside—that is the intermediate position (al-manzila bayn al-manzilatayn), which is the view of the Mu'tazila. However, they disagreed on the actions: according to Abu Ali and Abu Hashim, it is performing obligations and abandoning prohibitions; according to Abu al-Hudhayl and Abd al-Jabbar, it is performing all acts of obedience, whether obligatory or supererogatory, except that leaving iman and being deprived of entry into Paradise by abandoning a supererogatory act is not something a rational person would adopt as a madhhab. ... All of them (obligatory or supererogatory). And al-Juba'i and his son and most of the Basran Mu'tazila went to that it is the obligatory acts of obedience, both actions and abstentions, excluding supererogatory acts. And the Salaf (some of them like Ibn Mujahid) and the Ashab al-Athar (i.e., all the muhaddithun) said: It is the sum of these three: tasdiq by the heart, iqrar by the tongue, and action by the limbs. And the way of controlling (the classification) of these eight madhhabs is that iman, by consensus of the Muslims, does not go beyond action of the heart and action of the limbs. So it is either only action of the heart—which is ma'rifa (in two ways) or tasdiq as mentioned; or only action of the limbs—which is either the tongue (which is the two words) or other than the tongue (which is the work of acts of obedience, whether absolute or obligatory); or combined action of heart and limbs, and the limb is either the tongue alone or the rest of the limbs. Thus all the madhhabs are controlled by this classification.
Source pages p. 352

Commentary
This passage presents a systematic classification of eight madhhabs on the definition of iman. It begins by discussing the fourth view that iman includes heart, tongue, and limbs, leading to the Khawarij (who exclude the sinner from iman) and the Mu'tazila (intermediate position). The Mu'tazila themselves differ on what actions constitute iman. Then the passage enumerates the eight views: (1) ma'rifa (involuntary), (2) ma'rifa (acquired?), (3) tasdiq only, (4) two words (Karramiyya), (5) all acts of obedience (Abu al-Hudhayl), (6) obligatory acts only (al-Juba'i), (7) heart + tongue (view of some), (8) heart + all limbs (Salaf). The controlling principle is that iman, by consensus, is either an act of the heart alone, an act of the limbs alone, or both. The limbs are either tongue alone or all limbs. This classification captures all eight madhhabs.

عمل بالأركان

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think the Mu'tazila held a single uniform view on iman.
Correction: The passage shows they disagreed significantly: some limited iman to obligations, others included all acts of obedience.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the position of Abu al-Hudhayl and Abd al-Jabbar regarding actions that constitute iman?
A) Only obligatory acts and avoidance of prohibitions.
B) All acts of obedience, both obligatory and supererogatory.
C) Only the two words of shahada.
D) Only actions of the heart.

Key Takeaway
The view that iman includes all three actions leads to two major schools (Khawarij and Mu'tazila), with further internal division within the Mu'tazila.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If iman includes actions, what obligatory act are you neglecting today that might weaken your faith? Will you commit to performing it regularly from now on?
?
Do you ever judge others as 'outside iman' because of their sins, resembling the Khawarij? How can this lesson make you more cautious in passing judgment?
?
The Mu'tazila held a middle path—neither excommunicating sinners nor denying sin's effect. How balanced is your own view of sin? Are you too harsh or too lenient, and what change do you need to make?

The Salaf View and the Eight-Fold Classification of Iman
Passage presents the Salaf view and a controlling classification that yields eight madhhabs.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Salaf definition of iman as the combination of tasdiq, iqrar, and action, and how the controlling classification yields exactly eight madhhabs.

Core Idea
The Salaf see iman as all three, and a master classification yields exactly eight madhhabs.

After covering the Mu'tazila subdivisions, the passage presents the Salaf and Ashab al-Athar view that iman is the sum of all three components. Then the author provides a clever controlling classification that systematically enumerates all possible positions by considering combinations of heart and limbs, resulting in eight madhhabs.

Guided Path
1
Read the Salaf view
Read the part that states the Salaf (like Ibn Mujahid) and the Ashab al-Athar (muhaddithun) hold that iman is the sum of tasdiq by the heart, iqrar by the tongue, and action by the limbs.
This is the most comprehensive definition.
2
Understand the controlling classification
The passage then explains a controlling principle: iman, by consensus, is either an act of the heart alone, act of limbs alone, or both. This gives a classification tree.
Each branch leads to two or more sub-views.
3
Trace the eight madhhabs
Follow the classification: heart alone (ma'rifa in two ways, or tasdiq) → 3? Actually, the text says heart alone includes ma'rifa (2 ways) and tasdiq, but that might yield multiple. The limbs alone: tongue (two words) or other actions (absolute or obligatory). Heart+limbs: tongue alone, or all limbs. Count the total.
The passage claims this captures all eight madhhabs.
4
Verify the count
Check the enumeration: (1-2) ma'rifa two ways? But the passage says heart alone is ma'rifa (on two ways) or tasdiq → that might be three, but the author considers them as two types? Actually, the controlling classification enumerates as: heart alone (ma'rifa or tasdiq) → 2; limbs alone (tongue or other) → 2; both (heart+tongue or heart+all limbs) → 2; total 6? But the passage says eight. There may be additional subcategories. Reflect on the passage's claim.
This step encourages critical reading.

Proof from Verses and the Heart-Centric View of Iman
Author presents verses as proof that iman is in the heart; objection and response follow.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Quranic verses and prophetic hadith support the position that iman is primarily an act of the heart, and how an objection regarding the heart's centrality is addressed.

Core Idea
The heart is the locus of iman, but its mention does not exclude the tongue's role.

In this passage, the author presents a key proof for the Ash'ari view that iman is essentially an act of the heart (tasdiq), citing verses that explicitly associate iman with the heart. He then considers an objection: these verses might only indicate the heart's centrality, not that iman is exclusively a heart action. The response clarifies that while the heart is primary, the tongue's action is not negated. This lesson unpacks that argument and its implications.

Guided Path
1
Read the full passage carefully
First, read the Arabic passage and its translation without pausing. Notice the flow from verses to hadith to the objection and response.
The passage is dense; read slowly.
2
Identify the main proof
Look for the verses that mention 'heart' in connection with iman. They are used to show that iman is located in the heart.
The verses are from Quran 58:22, 49:14, and 16:106.
3
Examine the prophetic support
The author adds two hadith: the Prophet's prayer for a firm heart and his rebuke to Usama. How do these support the heart's role?
4
Understand the objection
The objection says: these texts don't prove iman is exclusively a heart action; the heart might be mentioned as the chief limb that influences the body.
This is a sophisticated critique.
5
Analyze the response
How does the author counter? He agrees the heart is central, but the hadith of Usama shows the heart's action is considered, not that the tongue is irrelevant.

لنا) على ما هو المختار عندنا ( وجوه : الأول : الآيات الدالة على محلية القلب للإيمان، نحو أولئك كتب في قلوبهم الإيمان ولما يدخل الإيمان في قلوبكم وقلبه مطمئن بالإيمان، ومنه ) أي ومما يدل على محلية القلب للإيمان ) الآيات الدالة على الختم والطبع على القلوب ) وكونها في أكنة فإنها واردة على سبيل البيان لامتناع الإيمان منهم، ( ويؤيده دعاء النبي الله ثبت قلبي على دينك، وقوله لأسامة : وقد قتل من قال لا إله إلا الله هلا شققت قلبه، وإذا ثبت أنه فعل القلب وجب أن يكون عبارة عن التصديق الذي من ضرورته المعرفة وذلك لأن الشارع إنما يخاطب العرب بلغتهم ليفهموا ما هو المقصود بالخطاب فلو كان لفظ الإيمان في الشرع مغيراً عن وضع التصديق قوله : (الأول) الآيات إلخ) لا يخفى أن هذه النصوص حجة على من يجعل الإيمان عبارة عن مجرد الإقرار اللساني كالكرامية وليس بحجة تفيد القطع بكون الإيمان عبارة عن مجرد كما هو المدعى لجواز أن يكون تخصيص القلب بالذكر في الآيات لكونه رئيس ،الأعضاء ومستتبعاً لما عداه كما دل عليه قوله عليه السلام ألا وإن في الجسد مضغة إذا صلحت صلح الجسد كله، وإذا فسدت فسد الجسد كله ألا وهي القلب»، وأما الحديث فيفيد اعتبار عمل القلب لا عدم اعتبار فعل اللسان كما لا يخفى .
For us (according to what we have chosen) are proofs: The first: the verses that indicate the heart is the locus of iman, such as 'Those are the ones in whose hearts He has inscribed iman' (Quran 58:22), and 'And when iman has not entered your hearts' (Quran 49:14), and 'his heart is at rest with iman' (Quran 16:106). And from that (i.e., from what indicates the heart as locus) are the verses indicating the sealing and stamping on hearts and that they are in coverings, for they are given as an explanation of their impossibility of iman. And it is supported by the Prophet's supplication: 'O God, make my heart firm upon Your religion,' and his statement to Usama: 'You killed a man who said "There is no god but Allah"? Did you split open his heart?' And if it is established that iman is an act of the heart, then it must be expressed as tasdiq, which necessarily entails ma'rifa, because the Lawgiver addresses the Arabs in their language so that they understand what is intended by the address; for if the word iman in the Sacred Law were changed from its linguistic meaning... His statement: 'The first: verses, etc.' It is not hidden that these texts are a proof against those who make iman merely verbal acknowledgment like the Karramiyya, but they are not a proof that yields certainty that iman is merely [tasdiq?] as claimed, because it is possible that the specification of the heart in the verses is because it is the chief of the limbs and entailing the rest, as indicated by his statement, peace be upon him: 'Beware, there is a lump of flesh in the body; if it is sound, the whole body is sound, and if it is corrupt, the whole body is corrupt—beware, it is the heart.' As for the hadith, it indicates consideration of the heart's action, not non-consideration of the tongue's action, as is not hidden.
Source pages p. 353

Commentary
This passage presents a proof that iman is an act of the heart, citing Quranic verses that explicitly mention 'heart' in relation to iman, as well as prophetic hadith. The author argues that since iman is placed in the heart, it must be tasdiq (affirmation) which inherently involves ma'rifa (knowledge), because the Lawgiver speaks in the Arabs' language. The passage then addresses an objection: these texts are a proof against those who equate iman with mere verbal acknowledgment (like the Karramiyya), but they do not decisively prove that iman is exclusively heart action. The specification of the heart could be because the heart is the chief limb that governs the rest, as the famous hadith says. Moreover, the hadith of Usama indicates that the heart's action is considered, but it does not negate the role of the tongue. Thus, the proof establishes the importance of the heart but does not exclude other components.

Correction: The verses and hadith show that iman is inherently tied to the heart, but this does not preclude the tongue's role; the heart is primary.

Key Takeaway
Quranic verses and hadith locate iman in the heart, but this does not exclude the tongue; the heart is the chief limb that governs other actions.

Two Quranic Proofs that Iman is Not Bodily Actions
Al-Isfahani presents the second and third proofs that iman is not bodily actions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand two rational proofs from the Quran that iman is not the actions of the limbs but rather an action of the heart, specifically assenting (tasdiq).

Core Idea
Iman is an action of the heart, not the limbs.

In this passage, the author strengthens the case that iman is an inner conviction, not external deeds. He presents two linguistic arguments from the Quran: one showing that iman is conjoined with good works (implying they are distinct), and another showing iman coexists with sin (proving deeds cannot be part of it). These proofs help clarify the true nature of faith.

Guided Path
1
Read the second proof
Examine how the Quran conjoins iman with righteous deeds. Notice the logical principle: a thing is not conjoined to itself or its part.
The author uses the Arabic grammatical rule of 'atf (conjunction).
2
Follow the objection and response
Consider a possible counterargument that the conjunction is for emphasis. See how the author rejects this as contrary to the apparent meaning.
3
Read the third proof
Notice how iman is mentioned alongside fighting (opposite of righteous work). Then study the verse about not mixing iman with injustice and its implications.
The author argues that iman can coexist with sin, so sin cannot be part of iman.
4
Understand the conclusion
The proofs establish that iman is an action of the heart. The author then distinguishes between tasdiq (assent) and ma'rifa (cognition), rejecting the latter.
This concludes the rational argument for iman as inner assent.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
( الثاني : جاء الإيمان مقرونا بالعمل الصالح في غير موضع من الكتاب نحو الذين آمنوا وعملوا الصالحات، فدل على التغاير، وعلى أن العمل ليس داخلاً فيه لأن الشيء لا يعطف على نفسه ولا الجزء على كله .
قوله : ( ولا) الجزء على كله فإن قلت يجوز أن يكون عطف الجزء على الكل هاهنا اهتماما لشأنه وتحريضاً عليه لكونه كمال الإيمان وسببا لترتب ثمرته عليه، قلت: ما ذكرته خلاف الظاهر لا يصار إليه ما لم يصرف عن الظاهر الذي هو خروج المعطوف عن المعطوف عليه فيتم البرهان .
( الثالث : إنه ) أي الإيمان (قرن بضد العمل الصالح نحو وإن طائفتان من المؤمنين اقتتلوا ﴾ [الحجرات : ۹] فأثبت الإيمان مع وجود القتال، (ومنه) أي ومما يدل على كونه مقروناً بضد العمل الصالح ) مفهوم قوله تعالى: ﴿ الذين آمنوا ولم يلبسوا إيمانهم بظلم ﴾ [الأنعام : ۸۲] ، فإنه يستفاد منه اجتماع الإيمان مع الظلم، وإلا لم يكن لنفي اللبس فائدة ومن المعلوم أن الشيء لا يمكن اجتماعه مع ضده ولا مع ضد جزئه، فثبت أن الإيمان ليس فعل الجوارح ولا مركباً منه فيكون فعل القلب وذلك إما التصديق وإما المعرفة والثاني باطل لأنه خلاف الأصل لاستلزامه النقل وقد عرفت بطلانه )
قوله : ( ومنه مفهوم قوله : الذين آمنوا (الآية الاستدلال بالآية على المدعى مبني على أن المراد بالظلم ،المعصية ، وقيل : المراد به الشرك كما روي أنه لما نزل شق ذلك على الصحابة رضي عنهم وقالوا أينا لم يظلم على نفسه فقال النبي وليس ما تظنون إنما هو ما قال لقمان لابنه يا بني لا تشرك بالله إن الشرك لظلم عظيم وحينئذ يجب أن يقال : المراد بقوله تعالى : آمنوا ﴾ [البقرة : ٩]، هو الإيمان الظاهري أي الذي يعلم منهم بحسب ظواهرهم من الإقرار والإتيان بالعمل الدالين ظاهراً على التصديق، وبنفي اللبس بالظلم أن لا يكونوا منافقين أو يقال : معنى ولم يلبسوا إيمانهم بظلم لم يكفروا بعد الإيمان ولم يرجعوا عنه
(Second: Iman came conjoined with righteous work in more than one place of the Book, such as 'those who believe and do righteous deeds,' and that indicates the difference, and that [good] work is not included in it because a thing is not conjoined to itself nor a part to the whole. His saying: 'and not the part to the whole' for if you said: it is permissible that the conjunction of the part to the whole here is for emphasis of its importance and encouragement for it because it is the perfection of iman and a cause for the arrangement of its fruit upon it, I say: what you mentioned is contrary to the apparent [meaning], and it is not to be resorted to as long as [the text] does not divert from the apparent which is the exit of the conjunct from the conjoined, so the proof is complete. Third: That is, iman was conjoined with the opposite of righteous work such as 'And if two parties of the believers fight' [al-Hujurat:9], so he affirmed iman with the existence of fighting, and from that is the meaning of His statement: 'Those who believe and do not mix their iman with injustice' [al-An'am:82], for it is understood from it the coexistence of iman with injustice, otherwise the negation of mixing would have no benefit. And it is known that a thing cannot coexist with its opposite nor with the opposite of its part, so it is established that iman is not the action of the limbs nor composed of it, so it is an action of the heart, and that is either assent (tasdiq) or cognition (ma'rifa), and the second is false because it is contrary to the original [principle] due to its necessitating transmission (naql) and you have already known its falsity.) His saying: 'and from that is the meaning of His statement: those who believe...' the verse. The deduction of the verse for the claim is based on that what is meant by injustice is disobedience, and it is said: what is meant is shirk, as it is narrated that when it was revealed, that was difficult for the Companions, may Allah be pleased with them, and they said: 'Who among us has not wronged himself?' so the Prophet ﷺ said: 'It is not what you think; rather it is what Luqman said to his son: O my son, do not associate partners with Allah, indeed shirk is a great injustice.' And then it must be said: what is meant by His statement 'they believe' [al-Baqara:9] is apparent iman, i.e., that which is known from them according to their external appearances of confession and performing work that outwardly indicates assent, and by negating the mixing with injustice that they not be hypocrites, or it is said: the meaning of 'and do not mix their iman with injustice' is they did not disbelieve after iman and did not return from it.
Source pages p. 354

Commentary
This passage presents two proofs that iman (faith) is not the actions of the limbs but rather an action of the heart. The author follows a logical sequence: first, the second proof from Quranic usage: iman is often conjoined with righteous deeds (e.g., 'those who believe and do righteous deeds'), and conjunction implies difference; a thing is not conjoined to itself or its part. The author then addresses a potential objection: one might argue that the conjunction of 'righteous deeds' with 'iman' is for emphasis, treating deeds as a part of the whole. He rejects this, stating it is contrary to the apparent meaning and cannot be accepted without clear textual evidence. Next, the third proof: iman is also conjoined with the opposite of righteous deeds, such as fighting (Quran 49:9), and the verse 'those who believe and do not mix their iman with injustice' (Quran 6:82) implies that iman can coexist with injustice (sin). Since a thing cannot coexist with its opposite or the opposite of its part, actions cannot be part of iman. The conclusion: iman is an action of the heart, which is either assenting (tasdiq) or cognition (ma'rifa); the second is false because it would require a transmitted basis (naql) not present. The passage ends with a discussion of the interpretation of 'zulm' (injustice) in the verse: it is either sin or shirk, and the Companions' concern is addressed by the hadith of Luqman, showing it refers to shirk. The apparent iman includes confession and actions that outwardly indicate assent, while true iman remains heart's assent.

Correction: The Quranic conjunction actually indicates that the two are distinct; a thing is not conjoined to itself or its part.

Key Takeaway
Iman is an action of the heart, specifically assenting (tasdiq), and not the actions of the limbs.

Faith and Disbelief

The Karamiyya Objection and the Response: Iman is Heart's Assent
Al-Isfahani refutes the Karamiyya claim that iman is merely verbal confession.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the Karamiyya's claim that iman is merely verbal confession is refuted, and why heart's assent is considered the essence of faith for salvation.

Core Idea
True iman requires heart's assent; verbal confession alone suffices only for worldly rulings.

The Karamiyya sect argued that iman is nothing more than verbal confession of the shahada. Al-Isfahani refutes this claim by showing that linguistically and Quranically, assent (tasdiq) necessarily implies heart's acceptance. This passage presents both the objection and a detailed response, clarifying the distinction between worldly and otherworldly rulings.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection from the Karamiyya
Understand why they think iman should be verbal assent based on language and practice of the Prophet.
2
Examine the first refutation
Notice the linguistic argument and the Quranic verses (2:8 and 49:14) that separate verbal confession from true faith.
3
Analyze the second argument and its response
See how the Karamiyya cite prophetic practice, and how the author counters with consensus and a verse.
4
Grasp the resolution
Learn the distinction between worldly rulings (based on apparent acts) and real iman (based on heart's assent).
5
Consider the reductio ad absurdum
The Karamiyya view would condemn a mute believer as disbeliever, which is absurd.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن قيل : فلم لا تجعلونه التصديق باللسان ؟ يريد أنكم إذا أثبتم النقل عن المعنى اللغوي وجب عليكم أن تجعلوا الإيمان عبارة عن التصديق باللسان مذهب الكرامية فإن أهل اللغة لا يعلمون من التصديق إلا ذلك قلنا لو كما هو فرض عدم وضع صدقت ،لمعنى بل كان مهملا ( أو ) فرض ( وضعه لمعنى التصديق لم يكن المتلفظ به على ذلك التقدير (مصدقاً) بحسب اللغة (قطعاً فالتصديق، إما معنى هذه اللفظة أو هذه اللفظة لدلالتها على معناها وأياً ما كان فيجب الجزم بعلم العقلاء من أهل اللغة ( ضرورة بالتصديق القلبي) فكيف يقال : إنهم لا يعلمون إلا اللساني ويؤيده قوله تعالى : ومن الناس من يقول آمنا بالله وباليوم الآخر وما هم بمؤمنين [البقرة : ۸] وقوله : وقالت الأعراب آمنا ﴾ [ الحجرات : ١٤ ] ، الآية فقد أثبت في هاتين الآيتين التصديق اللساني ونفي الإيمان فعلم أن المراد به التصديق القلبي دون اللساني، احتج الكرامية بأنه تواتر أن الرسول عليه السلام والصحابة والتابعين كانوا يقنعون بالكلمتين ممن أتى بهما ولا يستفسرون عن علمه وتصديقه القلبي، وعمله فيحكمون بإيمانه بمجرد الكلمتين فعلمنا أنه الإيمان بلا علم وعمل. الجواب : معارضته بالإجماع على أن المنافق كافر مع إقراره باللسان وتلفظه بالشهادتين و معارضته بنحو قوله تعالى : قل لم تؤمنوا ولكن قولوا أسلمنا ﴾ [ الحجرات : ١٤]، و حله بأن يقال : لا نزاع في أنه أي التصديق اللساني يسمى إيماناً لغة لدلالته على التصديق القلبي و لا في أنه يترتب عليه في الشرع أحكام الإيمان ظاهراً، فإن الشارع جعل مناط الأحكام الأمور الظاهرة المنضبطة والتصديق القلبي أمر خفي لا يطلع عليه بخلاف الإقرار باللسان فإنه مكشوف بلا سترة فيناط به الأحكام الدنيوية ، وإنما النزاع فيما بينه وبين الله النزاع في الإيمان الحقيقي الذي يترتب عليه الأحكام الأخروية ، ثم نقول لهم : يلزمكم أن من صدق بقلبه وهم بالتكلم بالكلمتين فمنعه منه مانع من خرس وغيره كخوف من مخالف أن يكون كافراً وهو خلاف الإجماع.
For if it is said: Why do you not make it verbal assent (al-tasdiq bi al-lisān)? He means: If you affirm the transmission (al-naql) from the linguistic meaning, you must make iman an expression of verbal assent – the doctrine of the Karamiyya – for the people of language know nothing of assent except that. We said: Even if it is assumed that 'sadaqta' was not placed for a meaning but was meaningless, or assumed it was placed for the meaning of assent, the one who utters it would not, on that assumption, be 'musaddiq' (a believer) according to language absolutely. For assent (al-tasdiq) is either the meaning of this word or this word indicates its meaning. In any case, it is necessary to be certain that the rational people among the people of language know by necessity heart's assent (al-tasdiq al-qalbī). So how can it be said that they know only verbal assent? And it is supported by His statement: 'And among the people are those who say: We believe in Allah and the Last Day, but they are not believers' (al-Baqara:8) and His statement: 'The Bedouins say: We believe' (al-Hujurāt:14), the verse. For in these two verses He affirmed verbal assent and negated iman, so it is known that what is meant by it is heart's assent, not verbal assent. The Karamiyya argued by the tawātur that the Messenger ﷺ, the Companions, and the Successors were content with the two words (shahāda) from whoever uttered them and did not inquire about his knowledge and heart's assent and his action; they judged him a believer by the mere two words. So we know that it is iman without knowledge and action. The answer: It is countered by the consensus that the hypocrite is a disbeliever despite his verbal confession and uttering the two shahādas; and it is countered by such as His statement: 'Say: You have not believed, but say: We have submitted' (al-Hujurāt:14). And its resolution is by saying: There is no dispute that verbal assent is called iman linguistically due to its indication of heart's assent, nor that it entails, in the Sharī‘a, the rulings of iman outwardly. For the Lawgiver made the basis of rulings upon apparent and regulated matters, while heart's assent is a hidden matter not known, unlike verbal confession, which is manifest without cover, so worldly rulings are attached to it. The dispute is only between him and Allah – the dispute concerning the real iman that entails the otherworldly rulings. Then we say to them: It follows from your view that one who assents in his heart and intends to utter the two words but is prevented by a preventer, such as muteness or the like, or fear of an opponent, would be a disbeliever, which is contrary to consensus.
Source pages p. 354

Commentary
This passage presents an objection from the Karamiyya and the author's response. It begins with the Karamiyya argument: if iman is not actions but heart's assent, why not make it verbal assent (pronouncing the shahada)? They claim the linguists only know 'tasdiq' as verbal utterance. The author refutes this by arguing that even if the word 'sadaqta' were originally meaningless or meant verbal utterance, the meaning of 'tasdiq' necessarily implies heart's assent among rational people. Two Quranic verses (2:8 and 49:14) are then cited: they show people verbally confessing but being denied true iman, proving iman is heart's assent, not verbal. Next, the Karamiyya present their own proof: the practice of the Prophet and Companions accepting verbal confession alone without inquiry. The author gives a two-part response: first, it is countered by consensus that hypocrites who confess verbally are disbelievers; second, it is countered by the verse 'You have not believed but say we have submitted' (49:14). The resolution: no dispute that verbal confession is called iman linguistically and that it entails worldly rulings (since the Lawgiver bases rulings on apparent matters), but the real iman for the afterlife is heart's assent. Finally, a reductio ad absurdum: the Karamiyya view would make a mute believer who cannot utter the words a disbeliever, which is against consensus.

التصديق القلبي
النفاق

الكرامية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Mere verbal confession of the shahada is sufficient for true iman and salvation.
Correction: True iman requires heart's assent; verbal confession only entails worldly rulings in this life, but salvation depends on inner faith.

Checkpoint
In the response to the Karamiyya, what is the reason given for why verbal confession (al-tasdiq al-lisānī) is sufficient for worldly rulings but not for true iman?
A) Because verbal confession is easier to perform
B) Because the Lawgiver bases rulings on apparent matters, while heart's assent is hidden
C) Because verbal confession is mentioned in the Quran
D) Because the Companions accepted it

Key Takeaway
Iman (faith) is essentially heart's assent; verbal confession alone is sufficient only for worldly legal rulings, not for salvation in the afterlife.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever felt spiritually secure simply because you say the shahada, while your heart remained distant from Allah? Let this lesson's distinction between verbal confession and heart's assent shake that false security. What step will you take today to ensure your heart's assent matches your tongue's declaration?
?
In what area of your life have you been 'saying we believe' but actually only 'submitting' outwardly—like the Bedouins in verse 49:14? Identify one specific relationship, habit, or thought pattern where your heart resists full assent. What would it cost you to surrender that to Allah?
?
If you were suddenly struck mute and could never again utter the shahada verbally, would your heart's assent be enough to save you? Your honest answer reveals what you truly believe about iman. If you doubt your salvation in that scenario, then this lesson calls you to strengthen your heart's conviction today—not out of fear, but out of love for the truth.

The Mu'tazila Proof: Obligatory Acts Are Iman (First Proof)
Al-Isfahani presents the first Mu'tazila proof that obligatory acts are iman.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the first Mu'tazila syllogism that identifies obligatory acts with faith (iman) and the Quranic verses used to support each premise.

Core Idea
The Mu'tazila equate obligatory acts, religion, Islam, and iman through a three-step syllogism.

The Mu'tazila argued that iman includes actions, not just inner assent. Their first proof is a chain of identities: obligatory acts = religion (din), din = Islam, Islam = iman. Al-Isfahani presents this syllogism with Quranic evidence for each link. Let's examine how they construct this argument and the verses they rely on.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic and translation, noting the syllogistic structure. The argument has three premises leading to a conclusion.
The passage ends mid-sentence due to the source break.
2
Identify the three premises
Find the three statements: obligatory acts = din, din = Islam, Islam = iman. Notice the logical flow from one to the next.
3
Examine the Quranic evidence
For each premise, note the verse cited and how it is interpreted. The first uses al-Bayyina 98:5, the second uses Al Imran 3:19, and the third uses Al Imran 3:85.
The third premise also refers to an 'exception' which is not fully preserved.
4
Reflect on the theological stakes
Why does this argument matter? If accepted, it would mean that actions are essential to faith, contrary to the Ash'ari position that faith is primarily inner assent.
This argument is one of several Mu'tazila proofs on this topic.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
احتج المعتزلة بوجوه : منها ما يدل على إثبات مذهبهم ومنها ما يدل على إبطال مذهب الخصم. القسم الأول أربعة : الأول : فعل الواجبات هو الدين والدين هو الإسلام والإسلام هو الإيمان، ففعل الواجبات هو الإيمان أما إن فعل الواجبات هو الدين لقوله تعالى بعد ذكر العبادة وإقام الصلاة وإيتاء الزكاة وذلك دين القيمة ) ، إذ لا يخفى أن لفظة ذلك إشارة إلى جميع ما تقدم من الواجبات على معنى ذلك الذي أمرتم به دين الملة القيمة، ففعل الواجبات هو الدين ( وأما أن الدين هو الإسلام فلقوله تعالى : إن الدين عند الله الإسلام ﴾ [آل عمران : ١٩]، وأما أن الإسلام هو الإيمان، فلان الإيمان لو كان غير الإسلام لما قبل من مبتغيه لقوله تعالى : ومن يبتغ غير الإسلام ديناً فلن يقبل منه ﴾ [آل عمران : ٨٥] ولاستثناء
The Mu‘tazila argued by [several] ways: among them what indicates the establishment of their doctrine and among them what indicates the invalidation of the opponent's doctrine. The first division is four: First: Performing obligatory acts is the religion (al-dīn), and religion is Islam (al-Islām), and Islam is faith (al-īmān), so performing obligatory acts is faith. As for performing obligatory acts being the religion, it is due to His statement after mentioning worship, establishing prayer, and giving alms: 'That is the religion of the straight [path]' (al-Bayyina:5), for it is not hidden that the word 'that' indicates all the aforementioned obligations, meaning that which you have been commanded is the religion of the upright community, so performing obligatory acts is the religion. And as for religion being Islam, it is due to His statement: 'Indeed, the religion with Allah is Islam' (Āl ‘Imrān:19). And as for Islam being faith, it is because if faith were other than Islam, it would not be accepted from its seeker due to His statement: 'And whoever seeks a religion other than Islam, it will never be accepted from him' (Āl ‘Imrān:85), and by the exception...
Source pages p. 355

Commentary
This passage presents the first of four proofs from the Mu‘tazila that obligatory actions are part of iman. The argument is a syllogism: (1) Performing obligatory acts is al-dīn (religion), (2) al-dīn is al-Islām, (3) al-Islām is al-īmān, therefore performing obligatory acts is al-īmān. Each premise is supported by Quranic verses. For (1), the verse from al-Bayyina (98:5) after mentioning worship, prayer, and zakah says 'that is the religion of the straight path', where 'that' refers to those obligations. For (2), verse Āl ‘Imrān 3:19 states 'Indeed, the religion with Allah is Islam'. For (3), verse Āl ‘Imrān 3:85 states that any religion other than Islam is rejected, implying that Islam and iman are identical; otherwise, one could seek iman under a different name, but only Islam is accepted. The passage ends mid-sentence at 'by the exception...' as the text is cut off. This is the first of four arguments the Mu‘tazila use to prove that actions constitute iman.

الدين
الإسلام
الإيمان
القيمة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Islam and iman are identical in all respects.
Correction: The Mu'tazila argue for identity, but the Ash'ari position distinguishes Islam (outward submission) from iman (inner assent). This passage highlights the Mu'tazila view that they are the same.

Checkpoint
In this Mu'tazila proof, which verse is used to support the premise that 'performing obligatory acts is al-dīn (religion)'?
A) 'Indeed, the religion with Allah is Islam'
B) 'That is the religion of the straight path'
C) 'And whoever seeks a religion other than Islam, it will never be accepted'
D) 'The Bedouins say: We believe'

Key Takeaway
The Mu'tazila argue that obligatory acts are iman through a chain of identities, each supported by Quranic verses, forming their first proof against the Ash'ari separation of faith and deeds.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you treat your obligatory acts (prayer, zakah, etc.) as the very core of your faith, or do you see them as mere routines detached from your inner iman? What would change if you saw them as inseparable?
?
If the Mu‘tazila are wrong and deeds are not part of iman, then why do you still perform them? Is it out of habit, fear of punishment, or genuine conviction? Be honest with yourself.
?
When you recite Quran 3:85 ('whoever seeks a religion other than Islam, it will not be accepted'), do you examine whether your own actions align with that Islam, or do you assume it only refers to other faiths? How does this verse challenge your daily choices?

The Nature of Faith: Refuting the Karramiyyah
Refutation of Karramiyyah claim that faith is mere verbal confession.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why true faith is inner attestation (tasdiq qalbi) and not mere verbal confession, through the author's refutation of the Karramiyyah sect.

Core Idea
Faith is inner conviction, not just words — words only prove worldly submission.

This passage tackles a fundamental debate: what truly constitutes faith (iman)? The Karramiyyah sect argued that verbal confession alone suffices. But the author, drawing on Quranic verses and consensus, shows that real faith is inner attestation of the heart, while verbal confession is merely a sign that carries worldly legal effects. Let's read the passage to see the full argument.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then the English translation. Notice the structure: definition, proof, objection, answer.
2
Identify the two Quranic proofs
Find the verses cited and see how they distinguish between verbal claim and true faith.
These verses show that verbal confession does not equal faith.
3
Understand the Karramiyya argument
Grasp the objection based on the Prophet's practice of accepting the shahada without inner investigation.
4
Analyze the refutation
The author gives multiple counterarguments: consensus on hypocrites, another verse, a resolution distinguishing linguistic vs true faith, and a reductio against the Karramiyya position.
Note the logical force of the reductio argument.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المرصد الثالث - المقصد الأول : في حقيقة الإيمان. فعل القلب ( قوله تعالى : ومن الناس من يقول آمنا بالله وباليوم الآخر وما هم بمؤمنين [البقرة : ۸] وقوله : وقالت الأعراب آمنا ﴾ [ الحجرات : ١٤ ] ، الآية ( فقد أثبت في هاتين الآيتين التصديق اللساني ونفي الإيمان فعلم أن المراد به التصديق القلبي دون اللساني، احتج) الكرامية بأنه تواتر أن الرسول عليه السلام والصحابة والتابعين كانوا يقنعون بالكلمتين ممن أتى بهما ولا يستفسرون عن علمه وتصديقه القلبي، وعمله فيحكمون بإيمانه بمجرد الكلمتين) فعلمنا أنه الإيمان بلا علم وعمل. ( الجواب : معارضته بالإجماع على أن المنافق كافر مع إقراره باللسان وتلفظه بالشهادتين (و) معارضته ) بنحو قوله تعالى : قل لم تؤمنوا ولكن قولوا أسلمنا ﴾ [ الحجرات : ١٤]، و حله بأن يقال : ( لا نزاع في أنه ( أي التصديق اللساني ( يسمى إيماناً لغة ) لدلالته على التصديق القلبي ( و ) لا في ( أنه يترتب عليه ) في الشرع ( أحكام الإيمان ظاهراً)، فإن الشارع جعل مناط الأحكام الأمور الظاهرة المنضبطة والتصديق القلبي أمر خفي لا يطلع عليه بخلاف الإقرار باللسان فإنه مكشوف بلا سترة فيناط به الأحكام الدنيوية ، ( وإنما النزاع فيما بينه وبين الله النزاع في الإيمان الحقيقي الذي يترتب عليه الأحكام الأخروية ، ( ثم نقول لهم : ( يلزمكم أن من صدق بقلبه وهم بالتكلم بالكلمتين فمنعه منه (مانع من خرس وغيره كخوف من مخالف ( أن يكون كافراً وهو خلاف الإجماع. قوله الجواب) معارضته بالإجماع إلخ المعارضتان إنما تتوجهان على ما نقلته من المقاصد ، وأما على ما نقلته من تفسير القاضي فلا .
The Third Observatory - The First Objective: On the Reality of Faith. [Faith is] an act of the heart (His saying, exalted: 'And among people are those who say, 'We believe in Allah and the Last Day,' but they are not believers' [al-Baqarah 8]; and His saying: 'The Bedouins say, 'We have believed'' [al-Hujurat 14], the verse. For He affirmed in these two verses verbal attestation (al-tasdiq al-lisani) and denied [true] faith, so it is known that what is intended by it is inner attestation (al-tasdiq al-qalbi), not the verbal one.) The Karramiyya argued: It is transmitted widely that the Messenger, peace be upon him, and the Companions and the Successors were satisfied with the two words (testimonies) from whoever uttered them and did not inquire about his knowledge, inner attestation, or action, but judged him to have faith merely by the two words; so we know that it is faith without knowledge or action. (The answer: It is rebutted by the consensus that the hypocrite is a disbeliever despite his verbal acknowledgement and his pronouncing the two testimonies; and it is rebutted by something like His saying, exalted: 'Say, you have not believed, but say, we have submitted' [al-Hujurat 14]; and its resolution is to say: There is no dispute that it (i.e., verbal attestation) is called faith linguistically, because it indicates inner attestation; nor that in the Law it entails the rulings of faith outwardly, for the Lawgiver made the criteria of rulings the external and stable matters; inner attestation is a hidden matter that cannot be known, unlike verbal confession, which is manifest without concealment, so worldly rulings are attached to it. Dispute is only about what is between him and Allah—the dispute is about the real faith that entails the rulings of the afterlife. Then we say to them: It necessarily follows for you that if someone truly believed in his heart and intended to utter the two words but a hindrance prevented him (e.g., muteness or fear of an opponent), he would be a disbeliever—and that is contrary to consensus. (The statement: 'The answer: rebutted by consensus, etc.' – the two rebuttals apply only to what he transmitted from the objectives, not to what he transmitted from the interpretation of al-Qadi.)
Source pages p. 355

Commentary
This passage defines the nature of faith (iman) and refutes the Karramiyyah sect's claim that faith is merely verbal confession. The author begins by asserting that faith is an act of the heart (fi'l al-qalb). He cites two Qur'anic verses: one about hypocrites who say they believe but are not believers; the other about Bedouins who claim faith but are told they have only submitted (aslama). These verses show that verbal confession alone is not true faith; rather, it is inner attestation (tasdiq qalbi). The Karramiyyah counter: the Prophet and the early Muslims accepted verbal testimony without examining inner belief. The author responds in multiple ways: (1) There is consensus (ijma') that a hypocrite who confesses verbally is a disbeliever. (2) The Qur'an itself distinguishes submission (islam) from faith (iman). (3) The resolution: verbal confession is called faith only linguistically because it indicates inner belief, and it carries worldly rulings (e.g., inheritance, marriage) because the Lawgiver attaches rulings to external, verifiable matters. But real faith—which matters in the afterlife—is inner attestation. (4) A logical reductio: if only verbal confession counted, then a person who believes inwardly but is prevented from speaking (e.g., by muteness or fear) would be considered a disbeliever—but there is consensus that such a person is a believer. The marginal note clarifies that the two rebuttals apply only to the version of the Karramiyyah argument he transmitted from the 'objectives' (al-maqasid), not from al-Qadi's interpretation.

إيمان (iman)
تصديق قلبي (tasdiq qalbi)
تصديق لساني (tasdiq lisani)
إقرار (iqrar)
منافق (munafiq)
كافر (kafir)
إجماع (ijma')

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That faith is merely verbal confession without inner belief.
Correction: True faith (iman haqiqi) is inner attestation of the heart; verbal confession is only an outward indication for worldly rulings.

Checkpoint
According to the author, what is the essence of real faith (iman haqiqi)?
A) Verbal confession only
B) Inner attestation (tasdiq qalbi)
C) Performing all acts of worship
D) Both verbal confession and actions

Key Takeaway
Faith (iman) is fundamentally inner conviction of the heart; verbal confession is merely its outward indicator for worldly legal purposes.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You can recite the shahada fluently, but when you examine your inner convictions, do you find any area where your actions betray your words? What would you say if Allah called you to account for your heart's true state right now? Commit to one specific change this week to align your inner belief with your outward confession.
?
If you were prevented from speaking the shahada due to fear or circumstance, would you consider yourself a disbeliever? Why or why not? This lesson says no—but does your heart truly rest on that inner conviction, or do you rely on outward rituals to assure yourself? Ask Allah tonight to strengthen your inner faith beyond mere words.
?
The hypocrites in the lesson had verbal confession but were condemned in the afterlife. Is there any hypocrisy in your life—a gap between what you say and what you believe in private? Write down one instance where you say one thing in public but your heart feels another. Repent and resolve to make your inner and outer self consistent, starting today.

Mu'tazila's First Proof: Faith Is Acts of Obedience
The Mu'tazila's first proof that faith is identical to performing obligatory acts via a three-step scriptural argument.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Mu'tazila's three-premise syllogism equating faith (iman) with performing obligatory acts (din/Islam).

Core Idea
The Mu'tazila argue that iman = Islam = din = performing fard acts.

The Mu'tazila present a scriptural argument to prove that faith is nothing other than performing obligatory acts. They chain three Qur'anic verses to establish an identity: performing obligations is the true religion, the true religion is Islam, and Islam is identical to faith. This lesson unpacks that syllogism and its supporting texts.

Guided Path
1
Read the Mu'tazila's syllogism
The passage presents three premises. Read them carefully and identify each one. Note the use of Qur'anic proof texts.
2
Identify the three key terms
Identify din, Islam, and iman, and how the proof links them. The final conclusion is that faith equals performing fard acts.
3
Examine the 'istithna' argument
The Mu'tazila use an exception from the story of Lot to argue that Muslim and Mu'min are coextensive, implying identity of Islam and iman.
This is a subtle linguistic argument based on the word 'ghayr' as an exception.
4
Reflect on the overall argument
Consider how each premise is supported by a verse. This is an example of tafsir used in kalam.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
احتج المعتزلة بوجوه : منها ما يدل على إثبات مذهبهم ومنها ما يدل على إبطال مذهب الخصم. القسم الأول أربعة : الأول : فعل الواجبات هو الدين والدين هو الإسلام والإسلام هو الإيمان، ففعل الواجبات هو الدين أما إن فعل الواجبات هو الدين لقوله تعالى بعد ذكر العبادة وإقام الصلاة وإيتاء الزكاة وذلك دين القيمة ) ، إذ لا يخفى أن لفظة ذلك إشارة إلى جميع ما تقدم من الواجبات على معنى ذلك الذي أمرتم به دين الملة القيمة، ففعل الواجبات هو الدين ( وأما أن الدين هو الإسلام فلقوله تعالى : إن الدين عند الله الإسلام ﴾ [آل عمران : ١٩]، وأما أن الإسلام هو الإيمان، فلان الإيمان لو كان غير الإسلام لما قبل من مبتغيه لقوله تعالى : ومن يبتغ غير الإسلام ديناً فلن يقبل منه ﴾ [آل عمران : ٨٥] ولاستثناء المسلمين من المؤمنين في قوله : فأخرجنا من كان فيها ﴾ [الأنعام : ٩٩] الآية ) أن كلمة غير في قوله : فما وجدنا فيها غير بيت من المسلمين ﴾ [الذاريات : ٣٦] ليست صفة على معنى فما وجدنا فيها أي في تلك القرية شيئا غير بيت من المسلمين إنه كاذب بل هي استثناء والمراد بالبيت أهل البيت فيجب أن يقدر المستثنى منه على وجه يصح، وهو أن يقال : فما وجدنا فيها بيتاً من المؤمنين إلا بيتاً من المسلمين فقد استثنى المسلم من المؤمن فوجب أن يتحد الإيمان بالإسلام، ( قلنا : لفظ ذلك ) في تلك الآية ( إشارة إلى الإخلاص ) الذي يدل عليه لفظ مخلصين لا إلى المذكورات ( لأنه واحد مذكر فلا يصلح ) أن يكون ( إشارة إلى الكثير والمؤنث ) فإن أكثر المذكورات مؤنث (وهو) أي جعله إشارة إلى الإخلاص ( أولى من الذي ذكرتم، والظاهر المطابق لنهاية العقول أن يقال من تقدير الذي أمرتم به أو الذي ذكر ( إذ فيه ) أي في كونه إشارة إلى الإخلاص ( تقرير اللغة ) على أصلها، وفي كونه إشارة إلى المذكور مطلقا إخراجها عنه (هذا) كما مضى، (و) أما المقدمة ) الثالثة : ( وهي أن الاسلام هو الإيمان فهي ( إنما تصح وتثبت بالدليل الأول (لو كان الإيمان ديناً غير الإسلام لأن الآية إنما دلت على أن كل دين مغاير للإسلام فإنه غير مقبول، لا على أن كل شيء مغاير له غير مقبول فالاتحاد بين الإسلام والإيمان إنما يثبت بهذه الآية إذا ثبت كون الإيمان دينا ) وفيه مصادرة، لا يخفى) لأن كون الإيمان ديناً أي عمل الجوارح الذي هو الإسلام في قوة كونه عين الإسلام فإثبات الثاني بالأول يكون دوراً من قبيل أخذ المطلوب في إثباته ولو اقتصر على منع كونه دينا، إذ هو في قوة أول المسألة أعني كون الإيمان عمل الجوارح لكان أولى، وأما قضية الاستثناء فإنها تدل على تصادق المسلم والمؤمن دون الإسلام والإيمان، ألا يرى أن الضاحك يصدق على الباكي ولا تصادق بین الضحك والبكاء فضلا عن الاتحاد ..
The Mu'tazila argued by [several] proofs: some indicate the establishment of their doctrine, and some indicate the invalidation of the opponent's doctrine. The first division comprises four: The first: Performing obligatory acts is the religion (din), and the religion is Islam, and Islam is faith (iman); thus performing obligatory acts is faith. As for that performing obligatory acts is the religion, [it is] due to His saying, exalted, after mentioning worship, establishing prayer, and giving zakat: 'That is the religion of the straight [path]' (al-Bayyinah 5). For it is not hidden that the word 'that' (dhalika) refers to all the preceding obligations, meaning 'that which you have been commanded [is] the religion of the straight nation.' So performing obligatory acts is the religion. As for that the religion is Islam, [it is] due to His saying, exalted: 'Indeed, the religion with Allah is Islam' (Aal-Imran 19). And as for that Islam is faith, [it is] because if faith were other than Islam, it would not be accepted from its seeker, due to His saying, exalted: 'And whoever seeks a religion other than Islam, it will never be accepted from him' (Aal-Imran 85); and due to the exclusion (istithna') of Muslims from believers in His saying: 'And We brought out whoever was in it' (al-An'am 99) [and] the verse that the word 'other' (ghayr) in His saying: 'And We found not in it other than a house of Muslims' (al-Dhariyat 36) is not an adjective, meaning 'We found not in it, i.e., in that town, anything other than a house of Muslims'—it is false; rather, it is an exception (istithna'), and what is meant by 'house' is the people of the house. So the excluded-from (mustathna minhu) must be estimated in a way that is correct, which is to say: 'We found not in it a house of believers except a house of Muslims.' So the Muslim is separated/excepted from the believer, so it is necessary that iman and Islam are identical. We said: The word 'that' in that verse refers to sincerity (ikhlas), which is indicated by the word 'sincere' (mukhlisina), not to the enumerated things, because it is singular masculine and cannot refer to many things that are feminine. And making it refer to sincerity is more appropriate than what you mentioned. The apparent meaning consistent with the Nihayat al-'Uqul is to say: from the estimation of 'that which you were commanded' or 'what [was] mentioned.' For in making it refer to sincerity there is a confirmation of the language in its original [usage]; whereas making it refer to the mentioned things in an absolute way takes it out of that [original usage]. This is as has passed. As for the third premise (that Islam is faith), it is only correct and established by the first proof if faith were a religion other than Islam, because the verse only indicates that every religion different from Islam is unacceptable, not that everything different from it is unacceptable. So the identity between Islam and faith is established by this verse only if it is established that faith is a religion—and that is a petition of principle (musadara), as is clear, because faith being a religion, i.e., action of the limbs, which is Islam, is in effect the same as being exactly Islam, so establishing the second by the first is a circle (dawr) of the type of taking the desired [conclusion] in establishing it. If he confined himself to denying that it is a religion, that would be in effect the first point of the question, i.e., that faith is action of the limbs, that would be more appropriate. As for the matter of the exception (istithna'), it only indicates the coextensiveness of 'muslim' and 'mu'min', not of Islam and faith. Do you not see that 'the one who laughs' is true of 'the one who weeps,' but there is no coextensiveness between laughter and weeping, let alone identity?
Source pages p. 355

Commentary
This passage presents and refutes the first proof of the Mu'tazila that faith (iman) is identical to performing obligatory acts (fard acts). The proof is structured as a three-step syllogism: (1) Performing obligatory acts is the religion (din), as the Qur'an says 'That is the religion of the straight path' after mentioning worship, prayer, and zakat—the word 'that' (dhalika) referring to those acts. (2) The religion is Islam, as per 'Indeed, the religion with Allah is Islam.' (3) Islam is faith, because the verse 'Whoever seeks a religion other than Islam, it will never be accepted from him' implies that anything other than Islam is not faith; and also the verse about Lot's people where 'house of Muslims' is excepted from general believers—showing that 'muslim' and 'mu'min' are coextensive, so Islam and iman are identical. Therefore, faith is performing obligatory acts. The refutation follows: First, the word 'that' in the 'religion of the straight path' verse does not refer to the enumerated acts but to sincerity (ikhlas), because 'that' is masculine singular whereas most of the acts are feminine; the commentator cites the Nihayat al-'Uqul to support this reading. Second, the third premise (Islam = iman) is circular: to prove it, one must already assume that faith is a religion (din), which is exactly what the Mu'tazila are trying to prove—this is a petition of principle (musadara). Moreover, the exception (istithna') in the Lot verse only shows that every believer in that context was a Muslim, not that the two concepts are identical; a simple coextensiveness does not imply identity, as in the example of 'the one who laughs' and 'the one who weeps'—they may apply to the same person but the actions are not identical.

Key Terms
دين (din)
إسلام (Islam)
إيمان (iman)
استثناء (istithna')

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) One
B) Two
C) Three
D) Four

Key Takeaway
The Mu'tazila use a chain of Qur'anic verses to argue that faith is none other than performing obligatory acts.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

This proof forces you to consider that seeking any religion other than Islam is futile (Aal-Imran 85). Is Islam truly the only religion you seek, or do you sometimes prioritize other 'religions' like status, wealth, or comfort? How does this verse call you to reorder your heart?

Refutation: Misreading and Circularity
The refutation of the Mu'tazila's first proof, challenging the interpretation of 'dhalika' and exposing circular reasoning.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand two key refutations of the Mu'tazila's syllogism: the word 'dhalika' refers to sincerity (ikhlas), and the third premise commits a circular reasoning (musadara).

Core Idea
The refutation exposes a misreading of the verse and a logical circle in the Mu'tazila's proof.

The Ash'ari response to the Mu'tazila's first proof attacks two weak points. First, the word 'that' in the verse does not refer to the listed acts but to sincerity. Second, the attempt to equate Islam and faith relies on assuming what needs to be proved—a classic petition of principle. This lesson examines these refutations and the counter-argument regarding the exception (istithna').

Guided Path
1
Read the refutation's first point
The refutation argues that 'dhalika' refers to sincerity (ikhlas), not the acts. Understand the linguistic argument based on gender and number.
2
Read the refutation's second point
The refutation identifies a circle: proving that iman = Islam requires that iman is a religion (din), which is the very claim being argued.
This is called musadara or dawr.
3
Analyze the 'istithna' counter-argument
The refutation shows that the exception only indicates coextensiveness of labels, not identity of concepts, using the example of laughter and weeping.
This is a logical distinction.
4
Synthesize the refutation
Combine the two points to see how they dismantle the Mu'tazila's proof.

Source Text
